This occurred during the Hellenic period in Greece, the spread of the Roman Empire elsewhere in Europe (e.g., AD 43 in England), the start of Confucianism in China and the beginning of Buddhism in India. (Catt and Maslin, 2012)"								Iron Age	3.2		"Bronze was progressively replaced by iron for the manufacture of artifacts from about 4.0 ka in Anatolia, 3.2 ka in Mesopotamia, parts of the Indian Subcontinent and eastern Europe, 2.8 ka in western Europe and China and 2.3 ka in Japan (Catt and Maslin, GTS2012)"								Bronze Age	5.3		"The earliest bronze artifacts, and therefore the local beginning of the Bronze Age, date from about 5.3 ka in Mesopotamia (the Akkadian culture) and north Caucasus (the Maykop culture), from 5.2 ka in the Aegean region, Egypt, Anatolia and Levant, from 5.0 ka in the Indus valley (the Harrapan culture), from 4.3 ka in mainland Europe, from 4.2 ka in Britain, from 4.0 ka in China (the Majiayao culture) (Catt and Maslin, GTS2012)"								Neolithic	8		"Later, the small early Neolithic settlements along the Euphrates developed into larger towns of the Urukian civilization.  The first of these dating from ~ 8 ka was probably the walled town of Jericho, which then had a human population exceeding 2000. About 6 ka, the much larger city of Uruk was built in southern Iraq; its buildings of kiln-fired bricks, often with internal walls adorned with mosaics, covered an area of 6 km2 and served a population probably exceeding 40 000."" (Catt and Maslin, GTS2012)"								Mesolithic-Neolithic transition	9		"Neolithic (8,000 - 3,000 BC): The New Stone Age is when people began to use ground stone tools, cultivate plants, and domesticate livestock; but before the use of metal for tools.  ""The start of the Neolithic is defined by the widespread initiation of serious farming methods, which strengthened the lifestyle based on long-term settlements, and led to early farmsteads and villages.  Later, the small early Neolithic settlements along the Euphrates developed into larger towns of the Urukian civilization.  The first of these dating from ~ 8 ka was probably the walled town of Jericho, which then had a human population exceeding 2000. About 6 ka, the much larger city of Uruk was built in southern Iraq; its buildings of kiln-fired bricks, often with internal walls adorned with mosaics, covered an area of 6 km2 and served a population probably exceeding 40,000."" (Catt and Maslin, GTS2012)"								Mesolithic	11.784		"By convention, the Paleolithic ends and the Mesolithic begins at the Pleistocene/Holocene boundary.  The term Mesolithic is used mainly in northern Europe; Epipalaeolithic is used elsewhere. The Mesolithic of most European areas was characterized by the production of large numbers of very small blades (microliths), which were often mounted singly on a shaft to form projectiles such as arrows, or in groups to form saw-like tools.  (Catt and Maslin, GTS2012)."								Late Paleolithic	40		"Blade flake tools (defines Upper Paleozoic) is from about 40,000 years to 10,000 years ago.  ""It was not until the late ice age cultures of the Cro-Magnon people and some of their contemporaries outside of Europe that long, thin, delicate blade flakes were commonly produced and used.""  (http://anthro.palomar.edu)"																																								Africa	:	Generalized African ages	Egypt	North Africa	West-Central-East Africa	South Africa																Generalized African ages	block	150	198/207/174		off							TOP	0		0								Spread of Islam	1.358		0								TOP	2.332		Top of Egyptian Dynasties								Egyptian Dynasties	5.15		0								Neolithic	9		"The period of prehistory when people began to use ground stone tools, cultivate plants, and domesticate livestock but before the use of metal for tools. It is the technical name for the New Stone Age in the Old World following the Mesolithic. In the Neolithic, villages were established, pottery and weaving appeared, and farming began."								Blade flake tools	45		"Blade flake tools (defines Upper Paleozoic) is from about 45,000 years to 10,000 years ago. The Ptolemaic period was a large-scale experiment in bureaucratic centralism and mercantilism. Egypt was ruled by Ptolemy's descendants until the death of Cleopatra VII on Aug. 12, 30 BC.  Click [ <a href=""http://www.touregypt.net/ehistory.htm"">History of Egypt</a> ] for more information (TourEgypt)."								Late Period	2.664		"Late Period (Integration Period):  A phase of Egyptian history, c 664-332 BC comprising the 26th-31st Dynasties, stretching from the end of the Third Intermediate Period to the arrival of Alexander the Great. Shabaqo (716-702 BC), the second ruler of the Kushite 25th dynasty, exerted Nubian influence by moving the administrative center back from Thebes to Memphis.  ""The Nubians fall under the Assyrians invasion. The Greeks help re-establish order. A renaissance in the arts of the 25th Dynasty shows a return to the Old Kingdom style.""  Click [ <a href=""http://www.touregypt.net/ehistory.htm"">History of Egypt</a> ] for more information.  (Source = TourEgypt, Word Chron (http://www.reference-wordsmith.com:80/cgi-bin/lookup.cgi?category=&where=headword&terms=Late+period)"								--	3.069		"Third Intermediate Period (21st-25th dynasties). Thutmose, Amenhotep, Ramesses dynasties.  ""Extreme prosperity and renaissance in art and building projects mark the beginning of this period. Towards the end of the 19th Dynasty the increasing power of the priesthood corrupts the central government. During the 20th Dynasty tomb robbing is done by officials. The priesthood becomes hereditary and begins to assume secular power. The government breaks down.""  Click [ <a href=""http://www.touregypt.net/ehistory.htm"">History of Egypt</a> ] for more information (TourEgypt)"								--	3.759		"Second Intermediate Period is the 13th through 17th Dynasties.  Amosis I, the founder of Dyn. XVIII in 1575 BC ends this period.  ""Both the First and Second Intermediate dark periods begin with a chaotic series of insignificant native rulers. In both, intruders from Palestine cast their shadow over the Delta and even into the Valley. Also in both, relief comes at last from a hardy race of Theban princes, who after quelling internal dissension expel the foreigner and usher in a new epoch of immense power and prosperity.""  ""The Hyksos invade and conquer. Eventually the Theban princes regain power. Kamose defeats the Hyksos."" -- reworded from TourEgypt - Second Intermediate Period:  Click [ <a href=""http://www.touregypt.net/hsecin1a.htm"">Second Intermediate Period</a> ] for more information."								Middle Kingdom	3.986		"Middle Kingdom:  A period in Egyptian history of the 11th through 12th Dynasties, c 1986-1630 BC. Prosperity and renaissance existed for a long period of time, but eventually, internal problems become apparent."" quote from TourEgypt - History of Egypt -- Click [ <a href=""http://www.touregypt.net/ehistory.htm"">History of Egypt</a> ] for more information."								--	4.184		"First Intermediate Period is the 7th through 10th Dynasties. DASHED: Little actual history is known of the pharaohs of the early dynasties. Their monuments, however, are some of the most studied artifacts in the world. A chronological phase in southern Mesopotamia between c 2900-2330 BC, ending with the founding of the dynasty of Akka. Ceremonial style palattes with relief ornaments (particularly Dynasty 0) Mastabas – rectangulat mud-brick superstructures above kings' and official' tombs. Engraved wood, ivory, and stone cylander seals of the “Mesopotamian style” (also in use in the Old Kindom). First annal: Wooden and ivory labels with engraved or painted signs recording important events of a year. Thin walled stone jars. Facon Horus as the main royal deity. {Sources: TourEgypt:  Pharaonic Dynasty 1 -- Click [ <a href=""http://www.touregypt.net/hdyn1.htm"">Dynasty 1</a> and <a href=""http://www.touregypt.net/hdyn2.htm"">Dynasty 2</a>] for more information.   Other sources:  Encyclopedia, Pgs. 77, 78; and Wordsmith -- Click [ <a href=""http://www.reference-wordsmith.com:80/cgi-bin/lookup.cgi?category=&where=headword&terms=Early+Dynastic)"">Early Dynasty</a>] ."								Naqadan Culture	6.5		"Upper Egypt Predynastic (Nagada Culture) -- Villages were located on the low terraces above the Nile floodplain and presumable on the floodplain and levees and sometimes along large tributary wadis.  In the Early Predynastic, burials in fetal position, wrapped in grass mats or skins and laid in simple oval pits with none to a few simple furnishings. Gradually, funerary became more elaborate, with the appearance of some graves with boxes and trays, rectangular mud-brick chambers, superstructures, and abundant funishings. Subterranean rock-cut tombs occur in some elite cemeteries.  There are several classes of handmade pottery that classify the various subperiods. Slate palettes were cut into rectangular and zoomorphic shapes and were probably used for grinding cosmetic pigments. Various hard stone were used to make disk and pear shaped maceheads and stone vassels. The stone vessels were manufactured in specialised areas and deposited in elite graves. ""Ripple-flaked"" flint or chert knives and ""fishtail""shaped blades were present. Some graves contained copper in the form of fishhooks and chisels, elephant and hippopotamus ivory was carved into figurines, amulets, combs and fetish objects.  Houses were circular, subrectangular, sometimes semi-subterranean with stone or mud-brick lined lower walls, ans sometimes rings of posts supporting skin-covered or plaited reed superstructures.  Excerpts from TourEgypt Feature Story ""Upper Egyptian Neolithic and Predynastic Religion and Rulers"" by Carolline Seawright -- Click [ <a href=""http://www.touregypt.net/featurestories/predynastic.htm"">Predynastic</a> ] for more information."								Bashendi pastorism	8.3		Bashendi A pastorism (sheep and goats).  Neolithic: bifacial core industry with hollow-based projectile points and denticulated sickles.								Masara	10.5	dashed	"Masara pastoralism.  The technology associated with this period are backed bladelets with end scrapers, perforators, burins, geometrics (in later phases only), pieces with continuous retouch, and denticulates as important tool classes. Levallois technology occurs as a minor element in most assemblages south of Luxor, with increasing frequency in the south. Limited work in bone, mostly simple awls and bipointed fish gorges. Use of red ocher for pigment.  Follows the Middle Paleolithic Egypt Tradition and precedes the Upper Egypt Predynastic and Lower Egypt Predynastic traditions.  "								Qadan	15		"Qadan (13,000 - 9,000 BC)  from the Second Cataract of the Nile to Tushka (about 250 km upriver from Aswan), actually have cemeteries and evidence of ritual burial. It is also during this time that the first great experiments in ordered agriculture began.  From TourEgypt ""Middle Paleolithic"" -- Click [ <a href=""http://www.touregypt.net/ebph2.htm"">History of Egypt</a> ] for more information."								Fakhurian	17		"The Fakhurian industry (17,000 - 15,000 BC) was based entirely on microlithic tools. From TourEgypt ""Middle Paleolithic"" -- Click [ <a href=""http://www.touregypt.net/ebph2.htm"">History of Egypt</a> ] for more information."								Halfan	20		"Halfan people, for it was much more than just a way of making tools, flourished between 18,000 and 15,000 BC on a diet of large herd animals and the Khormusan tradition of fishing.   From TourEgypt ""Middle Paleolithic"" -- Click [ <a href=""http://www.touregypt.net/ebph2.htm"">History of Egypt</a> ] for more information."								Upper Paleolithic	32		"Little is known about the period from 40,000 - 17,000 BC. The growing barrenness of the Sahara would obviously cause many of the settlements to die of starvation, and once again survival of the human race in this area depended on the Nile.  From TourEgypt ""Middle Paleolithic"" -- Click [ <a href=""http://www.touregypt.net/ebph2.htm"">History of Egypt</a> ] for more information."								Late Paleolithic (Aterian and Khormusan)	45		"Aterian Industry, named for the type site at Bir-el-Ater in Tunisia, began some time around 40,000 BC, about the middle of the pluvial, and ended just shy of 30,000 BC.  Aterian innovations included points for small spears characterized by a distinct plug on the bottom for a more secure fit and the spear-thrower. These new developments permitted increased efficiency in hunting large grazing animals.  The Khormusans with their small sharp points were also efficient hunters, as well as being gatherers and fishers, plus wild cattle (roughly twice as big as our domestic cattle), and fish from the Nile.  These two cultures, for such they were, existed almost side-by-side until the end of the pluvial.   Rrom TourEgypt ""Middle Paleolithic"" -- Click [ <a href=""http://www.touregypt.net/ebph2.htm"">History of Egypt</a> ] for more information."																																								Egypt Events	event	150	247/249/163		off	"Majority of information is from: TourEgypt - History of Egypt [Click  <a href=""http://www.touregypt.net/ehistory.htm"">website</a> ] -- a very detailed, well-documented, on-line source."					FAD												Gamal Nasser	0.048		"Gamal Nasser returns Egypt to self-rule by native Egyptians in a 1952 coup. [""Beware of Dynasty 0 kings' lists. Many such example exist, particularly on the internet, that definitively arrange these very early kings in some sort of order, such Crocodile, Iry-Hor, Ka, Scorpion and Narmer. The Gerzeans had mastered the art of agriculture and the use of artificial irrigation, and no longer needed to hunt for their food. The people started live in towns, not just villages, creating areas of higher population density than ever before.  They traded with far distant peoples for copper and other goods (they traded much further than the previous two cultures) - silver, lapis lazuli, lead, cylinder seals were some goods traded for from Asia and Mesopotamia.""  Excerpts from TourEgypt Feature Story ""Upper Egyptian Neolithic and Predynastic Religion and Rulers"" by Carolline Seawright -- Click [ <a href=""http://www.touregypt.net/featurestories/predynastic.htm"">Predynastic</a> ] for more information."								Badari (semi-nomadic)	6.5		"Naqada I or ""Old Predynastic"" Period (also known as the Amratian Period, beginning around 4000 BC (or, 4500 BC -- see last sentence).  The Badari of Upper Egypt are believed to be the ancestors to the predynastic Egyptians in Upper Egypt. They lived at about 4400 BC and may have even been as far back as 5000 BC. Though they were a semi-nomadic people, they started to cultivate grain and domesticate their animals.  Succeeding the Badari, the Naqada people took over. They were one of the most important prehistoric cultures in Upper Egypt, and their development can be traced to the founding of the Egyptian state.  The Amratian (Naqada I) started as a parallel culture to the Badari, but eventually superimposed itself on the other, and finally replaced it. These, though, were the race thought of as the first 'true Egyptians', and dominated between 4500-3100 BC.""  Excerpts from TourEgypt Feature Story ""Upper Egyptian Neolithic and Predynastic Religion and Rulers"" by Carolline Seawright -- Click [ <a href=""http://www.touregypt.net/featurestories/predynastic.htm"">Predynastic</a> ] for more information."								"Organized, permanent settlements focused around agriculture"	7.4		"Predynastic: begins ca. 5500 BC. Predynastic denotes the period of emerging cultures that preceded the establishment of the 1st dynasty in Egypt.  Coarse, handmade pottery of alluvial silts with great amounts of organic and inorganic temper, occasionally decorated with incising and paint. Stone vessels.  Chalcolithic: blade industry with “twisted” bladelets, blade knifes. Round and rectangular house structures, mostly in wattle and daub technique, later in mud-brick architecture, occasionally semisubterranean or subterranean, circular storage pits.  ""However, scholars generally divide this period further into four periods: [the oldest is] known as the Chalcolithic or ""Primitive"" Predynastic Period (beginning around 5500 BC).""  From TourEgypt ""Predynastic"" -- Click [ <a href=""http://www.touregypt.net/ebph5.htm"">History of Egypt</a> ] for more information.  And from Encyclopedia Word Chron - Click [ <a href=""http://www.reference-wordsmith.com/cgi-bin/lookup.cgi?category=&where=headword&terms=Predynastic"">pre-Dynastic</a> ] Follows the Late Palaeolithic Egyptian tradition, precedes the Early Dynastic tradition. "							LAD												Death of Cleopatra VII (absorbed into Roman empire). 	2.03		"Death of Cleopatra VII on Aug. 12, 30 BC. The Amarna Interlude, as it is often called, saw the removal of the seat of government to a short-lived new capital city, Akhetaten (modern el-Amarna), the introduction of a new art style, and the elevation of the cult of the sun disc, the Aten, to pre-eminent status in Egyptian religion. This last heresy in particular was to bring down on Akhenaten and his immediate successors the opprobrium of later kings.""  excerpt from detailed account at TourEgypt - Amenhotep IV (http://www.touregypt.net/18dyn10.htm)"												Khufu (Cheops)	4.528		Khufu or Cheops (2551-2528 BC) was the second king of the 4th Dynasty and was the builder of the Great Pyramid of Giza. The Holocene 'climatic optimum' came to and end, and it grew considerable colder. The fall in temperature was accompanied by the onset of a dry period in Northeastern Africa, and during this period the Egyptian climate was probably hotter and more arid than at present. "								Rapid drying of Sahara	6.5										Wet Sahara (no desert)	8.1										8.2 ka Cool event (Dry)	8.2										Wet Sahara (no desert)	10.5		"Around 12,500 B.P., a sharp increase in rainfall in East and Central Africa caused high floods downstream along the Nile. Soon this monsoonal rainfall began to expand northward, and there is evidence of limited rainfall in Egypt beginning around 11,000 or 11,500 B.P.  [ENTERED BY CAT]"								Dry Sahara desert	32		"Most of the Late Paleolithic in the Nile valley occurs during the period of the Last Glacial maximum  and the following interval of deglaciation. During the maximum, Egypt was cold and hyperarid with little or no local rainfall and extensive dunes of windblown sand accumulating in the desert and in the valley. Rainfall in East Africa was also much less, which reduced the flow of the Nile, estimated to have been less than 20% that of today."								Mousterian Pluvial (wet)	50		"The development of these new stone industries and survival techniques, coupled with the Mousterian Pluvial (which was even greater than the Abbassian that preceded it) between 50,000 and 30,000 BC caused a widespread distribution of early human culture. (from TourEgypt ""Middle Paleolithic"" -- Click [ <a href=""http://www.touregypt.net/ebph2.htm"">History of Egypt</a> ] for more information."								Wet/Dry cycles (see Note)	230		"The Middle Paleolithic appears in Egypt during oxygen-isotope Stage 7 and persists through Stage 4 or possibly the beginning of Stage 3. Many changes in climate occurred during this long period of almost 200,000 years. Isotope Stages 7 and 5 are seen as warm interglacials, where as Stages 6 and 4, which correlate with glacial maxima, are cold and dry. At various times during the Middle Paleolithic, there was considerable rainfall in Egypt, in some periods as much as 500mm per year, apparently during or at the onset of the warmer episodes. There is good evidence for six of these wet intervals in the Eastern Sahra, each separated from the other by periods of aridity. It is likely that during the driest periods there was little or no rainfall in Egypt, and only the Nile valley was occupied. "																																								North Africa	:	Sudan	North Sahara	Central Sahara	_METACOLUMN_OFF																	Sudan	:	Main Culture Interval (Sudan)																				Main Culture Interval (Sudan)	block	150	210/180/200									TOP	0										Historic period	4.65		"Same time as beginning of main Egyptian kingdom. Postdates the Early Khartoum tradition and precedes the historic period. Thin-walled, grit- or sand-tempered fine paste ceramics vessels, often with burnished exteriors and decorated with a wide variety of incised and impressed designs. Khartoum Neolithic ceramics tend to be less hard and not as well fired as Early Khartoum ceramics. Burnished ceramics also do not occur in Early Khartoum assemblages. The most popular ceramic decorations in the Kharoum Neolithic are impressed ""vees"" and zigzags. Other characteristic designs include horizontal and concentric semicircle incised and impressed decorations. Bone harpoons barbed on one side, with perforated bases, bone celts, and fish hooks. Disk beads carved from ostrich shells. Polished stone gouges and celts. Midden mounds ranging in size from 6000-40000 m2 containing lithics, ceramics, daub, bone and shell."								Saharo-Sudanese Neolithic	7.5		"The Saharo-Sudanese Neolithic appeared during a wet phase before 7500 until 4500 B.P. A first category of pottery in two sizes, widely open, conical bottoms, thick belly walls, rims, and handles, land-snail-quartz tempers, limited stamped decorations; and a second large category of undecorated bottles, with restricted necks, without handles, on conical bottoms. Ostrich eggshell bottles with a polar or an equatorial perforation and tortoise-shield cups were preserved. Knapped and polished lithic equipment included different rocks for knives, saws, as well as other things. Polished bone tools were well developed. Local and exotic bone materials, ostrich eggshells, and tortoiseshells were used to make some items. Volcanic rocks for axes and adzes played a decisive role for the emergence of a new social order. Through artistic pictogrammic expression emerged a written protolanguage. based on figurative representations, sending specific messages. Feminine-idol sculpture and peculiar engraved animal species bear evidence to specific local information selected from an implicit general communication between shepherds. Burial patterns of mixed Proto-Mediterranean and evolved Mechtoid populations were preserved inside caves and rock shelters.  "								Late Pleistocene- Early Holocene Maghreb	20		"Stone tools made from flakes and blades, frequently microlitic, characterised by backing; microburin technique for the production of geometric forms; probably heat treatment of raw materials; and frequent use of red ochre to coat implements in some regional subtraditions, as well as bone, occasional marine shell and ostrich eggshell ornaments and ostrich containers. Sites are open air and in caves or rock shelters and frequently contain large numbers of human burials. "																		North Saharan Interval	block	150	210/180/200		off							TOP	3		Top of the North African Protohistoric								North African Protohistoric	4		TOP of the Neolithic of Caspian								Southern Mediterranean Neolithic	7.5		Follows the Late Pleistocene-Early Holocene Maghreb tradition and precedes the North African Protohistoric tradition. 								Late Pleistocene- Early Holocene Maghreb	20		"Stone tools made from flakes and blades, frequently microlitic, characterised by backing; microburin technique for the production of geometric forms; probably heat treatment of raw materials; and frequent use of red ochre to coat implements in some regional subtraditions, as well as bone, occasional marine shell and ostrich eggshell ornaments and ostrich containers. Both points and tools are often made from Levallois flakes. Tabalbalat points are also common. Blade technology was used at some sites. Notes: A number of regional subtraditions have been proposed. Because of the extremely poor chronological control for the Aterian, it is difficult to know whether differences reflect time or space.   Relative time period: Follows the Mousterian in the Maghreb and the “Upper Lavalloisian” in the Eastern Sahara. These industries are both Middle Paleolithic or Middle Stone age, with tools made predominantly from flakes and with similarities to the Mousterian of Europe and the Near East. In the Maghreb there appears to be a hiatus between the Aterian and the Iberomaurusian, an Epipaleolithic industry. In the western desert of Egypt, the Aterian is thought to have been followed by another Levallois-based complex: the “Epilevalloisian” (Khargan, Sheikh Mabruk Units)."								Middle Stone Age	150		"The second part of the Stone Age in sub-Saharan Africa, dating from c 150,000-30,000 years ago and roughly equivalent to the Middle Palaeolithic elsewhere in the Old World.  (http://www.reference-wordsmith.com/cgi-bin/lookup.cgi?exact=1&terms=Middle%20Stone%20Age)"																		West-Central-East Africa	:	West Africa	Congo Basin	East Africa	Zimbabwe	_METACOLUMN_OFF																West Africa	:	Main Culture Interval (W. In some areas, there is introduction of material culture associated with Islam. None of these features are present throughout the entire region, and the diversity of localised patterns that characterised the preceding period continued. Follows the West African Iron Age tradition and precedes the historic period. "								West African Iron Age	2.5		"Defined by the appearance of indigenous metallurgy, which in some areas does not make its appearance until the very end of the period considered. Ceramic figures are found in some areas. Settlement mounds, ""defensive"" works, and stone tumuli are associated with soem local traditions. although these features generally date toward the later part of the period and are more expressive of the succeeding period of regional development. Follows the West African Neolithic tradition and precedes the West African Regional Development tradition. "								West African Neolithic	4		"Domesticated animals and/or plants were exploited. Material culture includes pottery in association with ground stone tools. Early on, the pottery is most frequently deocorated with comb or punctate or cord-wrapped cord impressions applied all over the outer surface of the vessel. Simple vessel shapes are common, with rounded bottoms and a high percentage of uninflicted rims in many areas. These are all elements that are characteristic of earlier Holocene ceramics in the Sahara. The proportion of microliths in West African Neolithic assemblages is highly variable, and there is evidence that large flake tool industries and prepared core lithic technologies increase during this period. Follows the West African Late Stone Age, preceeds the West African Iron Age traditions. "								West African Late Stone Age	13		"The presence of microliths. (Includes larger flake tool assemblages previously known as Tumbian, Para-Tumbian, Guinea Neolithic, Neolithic Hoe cultures, and Sangoan.)  In areas where good quality stone is available, microliths occur in a limited number of standarised, geometric shapes. In areas where poor-quality raw material must be used, the assemblage consists of small, unstandardised flakes. There were some larger stone flaked tools, in some cases Levallois technology. Ceramics appear in the Late Stone Age assemblages between c. 6000-5000 B.P. Early on the pottery is mostly decorated with comb or punctate impressions all over the outside of the vessel. Simple shapes with rounded bottoms are common. There is a high percentage of uninflected rims. Ground stone axes appear at approximately 5500-5000 B.P. Follows the Middle Stone Age and precedes the West African Neolithic tradition. "																																								Congo Basin	:	Main Culture Interval (Congo)	Congo Basin Events	_METACOLUMN_OFF																		Main Culture Interval (Congo)	block	150	210/180/200	notitle								TOP	0.5		TOP of the Central African Iron Age.								Central African Iron Age	2.35		"In Southern Cameroon and Gabon, the eariest Iron Age in broadly contemporary. with the recent Neolithic. In large parts of the inner Congo basin, the Iron Age appears to have been the first phase of Portuguese contact with the southwestern part of Central Africa, around A.D. 1500. Although the lower limit of the specified period is in accordance with the first appearance of iron working in the regions under discussion, the upper limit of 500B.P. is an arificial one. Tradional iron production in most areas here considered ended on in the colonial era. Attributes: Highly varied, often flat-based ceramics with generally rich decorations are characteristic. The most frequent ornamental techniques are rocking comb impression, incision, excision, stamping, applique, and rouletting. Lithinc implements include polished axes, hammer stones, grinding stones, and grooved and indented stones. Ornaments occur in the form of shell beads (Early Iron Age) or cylindrical ceramic beads (Late Iron Age). Diagnostic features include large settlement pits, shell middens, shaft-bowl iron-smelting furnaces, and pottery deposits in circular, deap pits (Inner Congo Basin). Engraved petroglyphs possibly dating from the Iron Age are found in areas with quartzite sandstones. The distinction between iron-using and iron-producing commuinties, although useful in some contexts, need not be made here because in Central Africa interpretations of evidence as belonging to the Iron Age almost exclusively rest on direct traces of the metallurgical process, that is, on the relics of furnaces, tuyeres, slags, and slag heap, whereas finished iron objects that could be indicative of exchange telation have not so far played a substantial role owing to preservation conditions. Follows the Central African Neolithic, precedes the historic period."								TOP	2.5		TOP of the Central African Neolithic								Central African Neolithic	4.9		"Follows and partly overlaps the microlithing late Stone Age in Gabon, the Congo, and Democratic Congo to the Tshitolian tradition. The Incipient Neolithic precedes the Iron Age tradition whereas the Neolithic proper is broadly coeval with the Iron Age.  West-Central Africa: Technology: Ground and polished axes, adzes or hoes of dolerite or schist, quartzite polishing stones, upper and lower grinding stones or quartzite or basalt, grooved stone, indented stones. Art and Pottery: Flat-based, richly ornamented ceramincs, often decorated with rocking comb impressions, zigzag incision, chevrons, herringbone patterns, festoons, punctates or appliqué. Vessel bases are frequently decorated, especially by rocked zigzag impression or incised motifs. Shapes include bilobed pots and vessels with angular sholders (vases carenes). These materials are characteristic of large and/or deep settlement pits. North-CentralAfrican: Megalithic monuments of rubble cairns with monolithic uprights and slab cists. Encyclopedia, p.46-58 (4,000/ 3,800 – 2,500)"																		Congo Basin Events	event	150	247/249/163	notitle	off						FAD												Tshitolian	14		"A tradition of core tools, especially core axes, along with small backed petit tranchet projectile points. Silver and gold were used to make ornamental goods and jewellery, as well as small figurines. Typical MBA ceramic forms include jugs, goblets, kraters, pithos, ""ring-base"" bowls, pot stands, and oil lamps. The so-called Tell el-Yehudiyeh Ware, named for the ""Hyskos"" site in the Egyptian Delta, includes small juglets with painted, incised, or ""punctate"" decoration, as well as ceramics in the form of fish, birds and humans. Pottery is usually wheel-made. Also typical of the MBA pottery assemblage is ""Leventine Painted Ware"". ""Monochrome Painted Cream Ware"" found primarily in the north, and was derived from a method linked to Amuq (Syrian) and SE Anatolia. Massive earthworks such as ramparts and glacis, as well as arched gateways made of mudbrick are also hallmarks of the MBA. The MBA is also marked by the first appearance of a local writing system (""ProtoCanaanite"")"								TOP	4		"Top of the Early Bronze Age (Early Canaanite Period, Proto-Canaanite, Proto-Urban)"								Early Bronze Age	5.5		"Early Canaanite Period, Proto-Canaanite, Proto-Urban.  The earliest phase of the EBA (Early Bronze Age) precedes the local appearance of bronze, which does not occur until later in the period. EBA remains are usually located in the basal layers of the major tells of Palestine, so broad horizontal exposures revealing architectural remains are limited. Information pertaining to the transition from the Chalcolithic Period to the EBA in the Levant are limited, yet there is strong evidence for some degree of continuity with the preceding Chalcolithic period.  The ceramics assemblage can be divided into cooking, storage, and serving wares, as well as separate groups of funerary wares. Common forms are storage jars, jugs, juglets, amphoriskoi, and “hole mouth” cooking pots and platters. Specific diagnostic features on pottery are the “wavy ledge handles” found mainly on jars, highly burnished red or purple-brown slip, and painted decoration particularly in the “line group” painted wares. The presence of Egyptian material, including the pottery and seal impression, is an important indicator of the EB1-2 in the south. It now seems clear that the Egyptian pottery (and the Egyptian presence) in the south ends in EBIb.  Diagnostic lithic types are few, but most notable are the “Canaanite blades”, though these occur in the Chalcolithic. Various metal weapons, especially the epsilon, or crescentic axeheads and elongated spearpoints, are also found in the EBA. "								Neolithic (Impressed Ware pottery)	6.85		Date = Encyclopedia plus 50; Source = Archeology dictionary (http://www.reference-wordsmith.com/cgi-bin/lookup.cgi?category=&where=headword&terms=Impressed+Ware)								Neolithic (pre-pottery)	9										Mesolithic (Epipaleolithic)	10.3																				General Mediterranean events	event		210/180/200	notitle	off						FAD												Constantinople taken by Islam	0.547		Constantinople taken by Islam in 1453 AD								Emperor Constantine converts to Christianity	1.688		Emperor Constantine converts to Christianity in 312 AD; and declares Constantinople capital of Christian Empire in 330 AD (Source = WebChron) 								Augustus becomes emperor of Rome	2.027		Augustus becomes emperor of Rome in 27 BC (Source = WebChron)  								Middle Bronze II c	3.65										Middle Bronze II b 	3.8										EB4 (Early Bronze 4/ Intermediate Bronze/Middle Bronze Age 1)	4		"Follows the Early Bronze Age tradition and precedes the historic period. There is considerable debate regarding the Middle Bronze Age chronology, and therefore a lack of consensus on both dating and nomenclature for this period. The confusion stems in part from the fact that the Middle Bronze 1 is regarded by many scholars to belong to a seperate period all together, which combined with Early Bronze 4, constitutes what is now commonly referred to as the Intermediate Bronze Age. Much of the Middle Bronze Age chronology has been based on contemporary Egyptian and Mesopotamian texts, in addition to a limited number of clay tablets from sites in the southern Levant, which constitute the first written documentation concerning Canaan and its population. "								EB3 (Early Bronze 3)	4.65										EB2 (Early Bronze 2)	5.05										EB1 (Early Bronze 1)	5.5		"Follows the Chalcolithic tradition and precedes the Middle Bronze Age tradition. After the conquest of Alexander the Great in 333 A.D. the Selucids brought Helenistic culture to the Amuq Valley, and founded Antioch (modern Antakya). Antioch was a hub of trade, culture, and wealth in the region. Then during the Roman Period, it became pivotal in the development of Christianity, until the Arab Conquest in 700 A.D. which brought Islam to the Amuq Valley. It was an important site during the European Crusades, and was eventually taken over by the Ottoman Empire in 1516 A.D. Today it is a part of Turkey, and the society is largely agrarian and contains an ethnically diverse population, as it often has in the past. IMPORTANT SITES = Antakya RESOURCE = The Oriental Institute of the University of Chicago"								Amuq Phases N-O	3.2		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = During this period, an independent kingdom sprang up in the Amuq Valley, after the fall of the Hittites and the beginning of a tumultuous period in the Mediterranean. Tell Tayinat was the capitol of this new kingdom, which showed a great deal of influence from the Hittite culture, making it a ""Neo-Hittite"" civilization. This Kingdom may have been known as Wadasatini. This kingdom eventually fell to the rulership of Assyria, who took tributes from the rulers of the Amuq Velley before eventually making it a part of their empire. IMPORTANT SITES = Tell Tayinat RESOURCE = The Oriental Institute of the University of Chicago"								Amuq Phases K-M	4		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = During this period, the capitol of Amuq was at the site of modern Tell Atchana, which at the time was probably known as Alalakh, which was the capitol city of Mukish, an Amorite kingdom, and vassal to Yamkhad (Aleppo) during the Middle Bronze Age, before becoming a vassal of the Mittani, and then the Hittite empires. Alalakh was destroyed around 1200 B.C. when the Sea Peoples caused a great disturbance in the Mediterranean. Amuq traded with Mycanean Greece, Egypt, Cyprus, Mitanni, Assyria, Babylonia, the the Hittites. IMPORTANT SITES = Tell Atchana RESOURCE = The Oriental Institute of the University of Chicago"								Amuq Phases H-J	4.75		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = This period is characterized by a change in architecture, as wel as the use of decorative hearths. The pottery of this period is characterized by the Red-Black Burnished Ware Pottery. Economy was still based on farming, however a large portion of the population was specialized in metallurgists, traders, and other crafts. Trade extended as far as Troy in Turkey and Ebla in Syria. IMPORTANT SITES = Tell Judaidah, Tell Tayinat, Tell Dahhab, Chatal Hoyuk RESOURCE = The Oriental Institute of the University of Chicago"								Amuq Phases C-G	7.9		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = During this period, the farming villages of the Amuq Valley grew in size, until they became towns. Social and Econimic orgamization was more complex, as evidenced by stamp seals from the period. Artifacts from this time began to show contact with the Halaf, Ubaid, and Uruk cultures of Mesopotamia. Agriculture became more systematic, and produce began to be stored. IMPORTANT SITES = Tell Kurdu, Tell Tayinat, Tell Judaidah REFERENCE = The Oriental Institute of the University of Chicago"								Amuq Phases A-B	8.8		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = This period begins what is known as the Amuq Sequence, which covers a series of periods in the Amuq Valley (known as the Plain of Antioch in ancient times) which is located on the Northeastern corner of the Mediterranean Sea. During this period, farmers began to settle the valley, raising cattle, sheep, goats, and pigs. The pottery of these levels (known as Dark-Faced Burnished Ware Pottery) is similar to that found in the early periods of the Anatoilian Plateau, Eastern Turkey, and the Levant. The people of the Amuq Valley traded with the Anatolians for obsidian. Metallurgy was decently sophisticated. IMPORTANT SITES = Tell Judaidah, Tell Dhahab REFERENCE = The Oriental Institute of the University of Chicago"																		Levant Periods	block	150	210/190/200	notitle		"A large amount of data for this section came from The Metropolitan Museum of Art's online timeline, which can be found at http://www.metmuseum.org/toah/."						TOP	0										Independent States	0.052										Ottoman Empire	0.484		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = The region remains important for international trade, with Baghdad remaining a center of religion and literature. Technology and Levant age = MET Timeline (for Balkan) "								TOP	5.5										Chalcolithic	6.5		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = Some new Chalcolithic developments can be seen in the establishments of formal temples and burial grounds, the emergence of craft specialisation and metallurgy, a dramatic growth in the human population of the region, and the division of sites into spatial hierarchies with settlement centres that coordinate social, economic, and religious activities. These formative developments had direct bearing on the structure of Levantine societies in the following historic periods. In archaeological terms, this Protohistoric period, dating roughly 6500 – 5500 B.P. is called the Chalcolithic period. Follows the Ceramic Neolithic tradition and precedes the Early Bronze Age tradition. For more information, visit <a href=""http://www.metmuseum.org/toah/hd/nahl/hd_nahl.htm""> The Metropolitan Museum: The Nahal Mishmar Treasure</a>. LOCATION = The Levant. The available evidence suggests that during the earlier half of the Holocene (around 10,000 – 5,000 B.P) conditions were wetter than the second half. In the Early Holocene, there may have been two wetter intervals (one around 9,000 – 7,000 B.P. and another at 5,500 B.P.) separated by a drier interval roughly between 7,000 – 5,500 B.P. This is shown primarily by oxygen isotopes from deep sea cores and the Dead Sea data. Thus, the second moist phase is centred around 5,500 B.P. and covers the very end of the Chalcolithic and the beginning of the Early Bronze Age.  Encyclopedia of Prehistory, Vol. 8, p. 56"								Ceramic Neolithic (Late or Pottery Neolithic)	8		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = Stone or mud-brick, mainly rectilinear architecture; lithic assemblages focused on flake production from pyramidal, multidirectional, or amorphous cores; rare and usually small symmetrical projectile points with notches, tangs, or both; domesticated sheep, goats, and cattle; clay animal and human figurines; wide repertoire of pottery, sometimes with dark burnished surface or geometric painted or incised decoration. Follows the Aceramic Neolithic tradition and is succeeded by the Chalcolithic tradition.  LOCATION = The Sinai, Levant, and the Euphrates and Tigris basins below the Taurus and Zagros mountains. IMPORTANT SITES = 'Ain Ghazal, Bouqras, Byblos, Hasuna, Jarmo, Jericho, Matarrah, Munhata, Sha'ar Hagolan, Tabaqat al-Bûma, Tell as-Sawwan, Umm Dabaghiyah, Wadi Rabah. Early in the Ceramic Neolithic, c. 7500 B.P., temperature and summer humidity may have dropped, making dry farming more difficult than in the Aceramic Neolithic. In general, the Ceramic Neolithic, experienced conditions somewhat more favourable than today, so that there were more perennial water sources, and woodlands were more extensive. At the same time, remnants of large Pleistocene lakes were shrinking in some areas.  Encyclopedia of Prehistory, Vol. 8, p. 40"								Aceramic Neolithic (Pre-Pottery Neolithic)	10.5		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = Stone or mud-brick architecture; lithic assemblages with high percentage of blade  or bladelet production; medium to large symmetrical projectile points; domesticated (in latter half) wheat, barley, peas, and lentils; managed  or domesticated goats; clay animal and human figurines; stone or plaster vessels, but pottery is rare.  The Aceramic Neolithic marks the beginning of village life in Southwest Asia. Aceramic Neolithic villages had many attributes of agricultural communities: large settlement size, substantial architecture long settlement duration, intensive  harvesting of seeds with sickles, equipment and facilities for storing and grinding seeds, and containers. Follows the Natufian tradition and is succeeded by the Ceramic Neolithic tradition.  LOCATION = The Levant, Syria, northern Iraq, southeastern Turkey IMPORTANT SITES = Abu Hureyra, 'Ain Ghazal, Basta, Beidha, Bouqras, Göbeckli Tepe, Jarmo, Jericho, Kfar ha-Horesh, Mureybet, Nahal Hemar, Nemrik 9, Netiv Hagdud, Qermez Dere, Tell Maghzaliyah The period began during the cold, dry Younger Dryas. About 10,000 B.P. , an increase in temperature and humidity left the lowlands frost-free in winter. Today, in most parts of Southwest Asia, seasonal precipitation is concentrated from November to April. There is some evidence that Holocene summers had rain, possibly because of a northerly route of monsoon winds. In general, the Aceramic Neolithic experienced relatively favourable conditions.  Encyclopedia of Prehistory, Vol. 8, p.1"								"Epipaleolithic (Upper Palaeolithic, Kebaran, Geometric Kebaran)"	45		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = Small, open air campsites of mobile groups of hunter-gatherers, with occasional stratified rock shelters and cave sites, almost all of the latter of which are located in the Mediterranean zone. Occupations commonly contain hearths; other features include rare, lightly constructed shelters and pits. Blade, bladelet, and flake chipped stone tools are the main material remains, including points, scrapers, burins, and microliths. There are sometimes accompanied by bone tools, ochre, and shell bead ornaments. Grinding and pounding equipment on limestone and basalt first appear during the Upper Palaeolithic, and increase in quantity and diversity during the course of the Epipaleolithic. Follows the Middle Palaeolithic tradition, overlaps with the Natufian tradition in Levant, and precedes the Aceramic Neolithic tradition.  LOCATION = The Levant and Mesopotamia. IMPORTANT SITES = Abu Hureyra, Abu Noshra, Avdat area (Boqer Tachtit, Boqer, Ein Aqev, Ein Aqev East, Rosh Zin); Ein Gev, El-Kowm (Nadaouiyeh, Umm el Tlel), El-Wad, Eyan (Mallaha), Gebel Maghara (Mushabi, Lagama), Har Harif (Rosh Horesha, Saflulim, Abu Salem, Ramat Harif), Hayonim Cave and Terrace, Kebara Cave, Ksar Akil, Lower Jordan Valley (Fazael, Urkan e-Rubb, Salibiya), Nahal Hadera (Hefziba), Nahal Oren, Neve David, Ohalo II, Qadesh Barnea, Tor Hamar, Wadi Hammeh, Wadi Hasa (Ain Buhira, Thalab al-Buhira, Tor Sadif, Tor al-Tareeq, Ucagizli, Yutil el-Hasa), Wadi Jilat/Azaq (Jilat 6, Uwaynid, Kharaneh IV), Western Negev Dunes (Azariq, Nizzana, Nahal Lavan, Shunera, Nahal Sekher), Yabrud II-III, Zarzi. The Epipaleolithic tradition extends across a period of dramatic climatic change. The tradition begins during the last glacial maximum when the Levant and Mesopotamia were much cooler and drier than today. Over time the climate ameliorated, and by ca. 12,000 B.P., a significant warming trend led to almost ideal conditions for human habitation. By ca. 10,800 B.P., the cooler and drier conditions of the Younger Dryas tool hold of the region, creating the diverse mosaic of environments and microenvironments we see today. Rainfall today varies from over 1000mm to less than 50mm annually, and is generally limited to winter months. Summer across the region are relatively hot and dry.  Encyclopedia of Prehistory, Vol. 8, p. 122 – 123 "																		Levant other periods	block	150	210/190/200	notitle	off	"A large amount of data for this section came from The Metropolitan Museum of Art's online timeline, which can be found at http://www.metmuseum.org/toah/."						TOP	6.2										Ubaid culture	7		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = This culture began in southern Mesopotamia, and then spread to the rest of Mesopotamia. During this time, the central government became stronger, and Zoroastrianism was made the state religion. The Sasanian Empire was situated between China and the Romano-Byzantine Empires, all three of which were linked by the silk road. The Sasanian empire took advantage of being in the center of this large trade route, and grew wealthy. Administration was strong enough to sponsor state run hospitals, schools, colleges, irrigation projects, and careful city planning. After this successful conquest they were known as the Parthians. Their empire equalled Rome in wealth, and lasted for almost 400 years. Their capitol was just south of modern Baghdad, called Ctesiphon. The culture was a mix of earlier influences, the most important being those of Sumer and Akkad. The spoken language was a dialect of Akkadian, however the written language was a script from Sumer. In Assyria there were 3 fairly autonomous city states: Nineveh, Ashur, and Arbela. Not much is known about Nineveh and Arbela during this period, however we know that Ashur was ruled by a series of vice regents who engaged in many building projects over the centuries, such as temples, palaces, and walls. They also participated in a profitable copper trade with Anatolia. REFERENCE = The Penguin Encyclopedia of Ancient Civilizations, p. 78, 90-92, 101-102"								TOP	4.17										Akkadian	4.35		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = ""The Akkadian Empire was the first ancient Semitic empire of Mesopotamia, centered in the city of Akkad. The Akkadian period is generally dated to 2350–2170 BC according to the Middle Chronology, which fixes the reign of Hammurabi to 1792–1750 BCE and the sack of Babylon to 1595 BCE."" Period specific characteristics appear in almost all cultural areas, including pottery and alphabet styles. Cuniform gradually replaced Sumerian, and was used for the Akkadian language. Akkadian imperial standard metrology (length,land, capacity, weight), was utilized throughout the Empire, and was even expressed in standard mudbrick and ration bowl sizes. Akkadian follows the Early Dynastic period and precedes the Ur III period. The periodization of the Empire over time reflects its politicoeconomic development: 1.)early Akkadian, the period of Sargon and his first two dynastic successors who conducted long-distance raiding; 2.) the reigns of Naram-Sin and Shar-kali-sharri, who built imperial regional fortresses and administrative centres; and 3.) the period of “confusion,” minor rulers, and collapse. . LOCATION: The empire controlled both irrigation agriculture in northern Mesopotamia (Sumer) and rain-fed agriculture in northern Mesopotamia (Subir). The latter extended from Zagros piedmont on the east to the westernmost drainage of the Habur River, midway to the Balikh River. The Akkadian capital, Akkade, remains unlocated. Important sites: Brak, Leilan, Nineveh, Nuzi, Sus, Ur, among others. . REFERENCE = REFERENCE = Encyclopedia of Prehistory, Vol. 8, p. 21"								Sumerian	4.9		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = Early Dynastic Period Mesopotamia (Sumerian)/ Ninevite V Period = This period shows marked urbanization, with most of the population living in walled cities which each functioned as a political unit, having their own rulers and patron deities. This period is also characterized by cuneiform writing and plain, wheel-made ceramics with conical bowls. Follows the Jamdat Nasr tradition and precedes the Akkadian tradition. The early Dynastic tradition is typically divided into three major phases: Early Dynastic I, in which the main features of the tradition are established; a transitional period called Early Dynastic II; and Early Dynastic III, in which written records become common and the Sumerian empire becomes well established. The tradition ends with the conquest of Sumer by Sargon of Agade.. LOCATION = The lower Tigris, Euphrates, and Diyala river valles in what is today Iraq. IMPORTANT SITES = Abu Salabikh, al-Ubaid, Girsu (Tello), Lagash (al-Hiba), Nippur, Tell Asmar, Tell Agrab, Ur (Tell al-Muqayyar, Uruk  (Warka).. ENVIRONMENT = Climatic conditions in the Early Dynastic tradition were similar to those today. Winters were mild, while summers were hot and dry. Mean annual temperature was between 20 – 25°C, and mean annual rainfall is only about 200mm. These conditions were stable to about 2300 B.C., when an abrupt climate change, noted in globally distributed lake, marine, and speleotherm cores, reduced precipitation to c. 20 – 30% from today's levels. The threefold dust spike that accompanied this abrupt climate change was not reduced to Holocene background levels until ca. 1900 B.C. The abrupt change was synchronous with 1.) the Akkadian imperial collapse, as well as the collapse of sedentary systems in Anatolia, Palestine, Greece and the Aegean, and Egypt. 2.) Amorite pastoral nomad movements that challenged state political authorities, as did “The Sandcrossers” in Egypt.  . REFERENCE = REFERENCE = Encyclopedia of Prehistory, Vol. 8, p. 112/ The Oriental Institute of the University of Chicago"								Uruk period	6		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = Late Chalcolithic (Uruk period) = In the larger region, there are a number of diagnostic types of pottery, seal design, and architecture associated with subregional areas. “Late Chalcolithic” usually refers to local cultural material in the steppes and foothills of northern and eastern Mesopotamia, as opposed to the “Uruk”, which refers to the material diagnostic of the southern alluvium of modern Iraq and southwestern Iran (Susiana). In the Late Chalcolithic material, there are further subdivisions into the eastern piedmont of Iraq, the steppes of Syria and Iraq, the western plains of Syria and the Hatay in Turkey (Amuq F), the foothills of the Taurus mountains in Turkey, and the foothills of the Zagros mountains in western Iran. This period begins in the Terminal Ubaid Period and ends with the earliest Early Bronze Age. The latter is known also as the Kurban V, Jemdet Nasr, Proto-Elamite, or the earliest Ninevite V period. It therefore fits in the Chalcolithic post-Neolithic period, whose earlier phases are the Halaf and Ubaid periods.REFERENCE = Encyclopedia of Prehistory, Vol. 8, p. 261 – 262. LOCATION = This period is notable in the basins of the Tigris and Euphrates rivers of the Middle East and their tributaries. Geographically, it covere the modern countries of Iraq, western Iran, Syria (all but the Levantine coast), and southeastern Turkey. IMPORTANT SITES = South: Ur, Uruk, Girsu, Eridu, Nippur, Abu Salabikh, Susa, Choga Mish, Farakhabad, Sharafabad. Piedmont and Zagros hills: Eshnunna, Rubeideh, Godin Tepe, Nuzi (Yorgan Tepe), Qalinj Agha, Tepe Giyan, Sialk, Geoy Tepe, Tepe Gawra, Nivevah, Mohammad Arab. Jazira and Habur plains: Teluh eth Thalathat, Tell al-Hawa, Hamoukar, Leilan, Grai Resh, Tell Azzo, Tell Brak. Middle-Upper Euphrates plains: Qraya, Hammam et Turkman, Hadid, Umm Qseir, Sheikh Hassan, Jebel Aruda, El Kowm, Habuba Kabira, Carchemish, Jerablus Tahtai, Tell 'Abir, Tilbes Hoyuk, Kurban Hoyuk, Samsat, Kazane. Taurus Hills: Arslantepe, Tepecik, Norsuntepe. Amuq and adjoining plains: Judeideh, Sakce Gozu, Karatut Merkii.. ENVIRONMENT = In the southern alluvium, temperatures in mid-afternoon vary from approximately 17-22C in November through early March to 34-35C  from late March to October. The climate of the region is semiarid to arid. The alluvial plains, steppes and piedmont are part of the desertic Saharo-Sindian vegetation zone: the Zagros and Taurus foothills fall in the continental Irano-Turanian zone. Rainfall in the winter growing season is at a bare minimum for rainfall agriculture. Most agriculture and water use depend on the rivers, whose flood is determined by snowfall in the Taurus and Zagros mountains. The flow rates vary from destructive flooding in some years to drought in others. In the northern plains and foothills, the range of temperatures is similar to that of the southern alluvium. Rainfall agriculture is possible throughout the northern steppes and foothills. . REFERENCE = REFERENCE = Encyclopedia of Prehistory, Vol. 8, p. 261 – 262"								Ubaid 	7.1		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = Hard-filed ceramic vessels consisting of buff or red paste and decorated with geometric designs in black or red paint. Other distinctive objects include bent-clay nails, clay sickles, and reptilian headed figurines. Architectural features include temples and tripartite structures in which large, frequently T-shaped hall is flanked by a series of rooms on each side. Follows the Aceramic Neolithic tradition and precedes the Late Chalcolithic Mesopotamia tradition. In Mesopotamia, the Ubaid represents the earliest known culture. In central Mesopotamia and Khuzistan, it follows the Samarran period and precedes the Uruk period. In northern Mesopotamia, it follows the Halaf period, which it replaces. It precedes the late Chalcolithic period, represented by Tepe Gawra, which is closesly related to the Uruk tradition of southern Mesopotamia.  [Ubaid divisions made equal within Ubaid interval for now.]. LOCATION = Iraq and western Iran, in what are referred to as Mesopotamia and Khuzistan, respectively. The earliest known sites extend from southern Mesopotamia and Khuzistan to as far north as the Hamrin basin in east-central Iraq. IMPORTANT SITES = Tell el-'Oueili. ENVIRONMENT = The Ubaid period was characterised by major climatic changes. During the beginning of this period, summers would have been somewhat hotter than today. Winter temperatures would have been colder. The beginning of the Ubaid tradition was also marked by a wet monsoonal climatic regime, with rainfall beginning earlier in the fall and ending later in the spring. By the end of the Ubaid tradition, climatic conditions were well on their way toward modern values, with perhaps slightly higher annual temperatures and greater aridity at the end of the Ubaid than today. In northern Mesopotamia, annual seasonality appears to have been extreme. Summer were intensely arid. During the winter, violent rainstorms resulted in erosion of dry soils, incision of wadis, and possible destruction of settlements.  REFERENCE = Encyclopedia of Prehistory, Vol. 8, p. 372 - 373/ The Oriental Institute of the University of Chicago"								Halafian	8.1		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = ""The Halaf culture is a prehistoric period which lasted between about 6100 and 5100 BCE"" (Wikipedia entry).  Architectural features include tholoi, buildings with circular rooms and rectangular anterooms. They were made with Tauf construction (using compressed mud) , and stone foundations. At Arpachiyah, it seems likely that the tholoi were domed, and over time they grew in size so that the most recent had central chambers 10 meters across. The Halafian is also known for very well made and richly decorated painted pottery, which was handmade. Anthropomorphic figurines, stone stamp seals, obsidian tools, and clay sling bullets have also been found. This period overlaps with the end of Hassuna/Samarra tradition and start of Ubaid tradition. According to the Oriental Institute of the University of Chicago, this period dates from 5600 to 5000 B.C.. LOCATION = Core Halafian area is rolling hilly and arable country in a broad band of territory across northern Mesopotamia, from Hamrin region of central-east Iraq through northern Iraq, across northern Syria, and into southern Anatolia, closely following the modern 250mm isohyet. Important sites: Arpachiyah, Girikihaciayan, Tell Hassan, Tell Sabi Abyad, Umm Qseir, Yarim Tepe.. ENVIRONMENT = There is evidence for a period of aridification immediately before the Halafian period, which may help explain the lack of continuity in settlement patterns from Hassuna/Samarra to Halafian. Otherwise, temperatures and rainfall are believed to have been similar to those of today, with average rainfall of 250mm through the core Halafian region, average winter temperatures around 5°F, and average summer temperatures of 80 – 90°F. REFERENCE = Encyclopedia of Prehistory, Vol. 8, p. 138 Top at 7000 B.P./ Beginning age taken from MET Timeline/ The Oriental Institute of the University of Chicago"								Samarra and Hassuna cultures	8.2		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = Named for the site of Hassuna where a particular type of potery was first identified. The pottery is decorated with incised lines and is lightly fired. The site of Hassuna was originally occupied by campsites before structures were built. Houses were made of pressed mud, and were rectangular, with smaller room surrounding a courtyard. Graves are often of children or adolescents who were buired under the floors of houses. Pottery found at the site of Samarra is characterized by elaborate geometric designs in many colors. Samarran houses were very regular in shape and size, and after demolition, new houses were built in the same location as the old houses, which may be an indication of land ownership. The Samarran economy was based in agriculture, and there is evidence they used irrigation. Burials are common, and many contain grave goods. The Hassuna culture lasted from 6200-5700 B.C. and the Samarra culture lasted from 6100-5500 B.C. For more information, visit <a href=""http://www.metmuseum.org/toah/ht/?period=02&region=wam#/Key-Events""> Metropolitan Museum of Art: Mesopotamia, 8000-2000 B.C.</a> . IMPORTANT SITES: Samarra, Hassuna. REFERENCE = MET timeline/ The Oriental Institute of the University of Chicago"								TOP	9.5										Natufian	12.5	dashed	"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = Natufian (Late Epipaleolithic) = ""The Natufian culture is the name given to the sedentary hunter-gatherers living in the Levant region of the near east between about 12,500 and 9500 years ago.  The term ""Natufian"" was coined by Dorothy Garrod who studied the Shuqba cave in Wadi an-Natuf, in the western Judean Mountains. "" (wikipedia entry).  During this period sedentary hamlets are located near stands of wild cereals and nut-bearing trees in the Mediterranean zone. This period is characterized by ground stone implements such as mortars and pestles for processing cereals and nuts and sickle blades for harvesting cereals. The dead were buried in formal cemetaries. Many utensils of this time were decorated. In the more marginal desert zone, more mobile adaptations are typical. This period follows the Epipaleolithic tradition and precedes the Aceramic Neolithic tradition. . Location: The Levant Important sites: Abu Salem, Ain Mallaha (Eynan), Beidha, el-Wad, Erq el-Ahmar, Hayonim Cave and Terrace, Nahal Oren, Ramat Harif (G8), Rosh Zin, Rosh Horesha, Saiide II, Upper Besor 6, Tell Abu Hureyra, Wadi Hammeh 27.. ENVIRONMENT = The beginning of the Natufian tradition corresponds to the peak of significant climatic amelioration at the end of the last glacial maximum. The Natufian period is marked by a time of steadily rising precipitation and termperatures, lasting through to about 10,800 B.P. and followed by an abrupt deterioration of the climate corresponding to the Younger Dryas event. Temperatures changes brought about with them changes in plant communities. The range of cereals like wheat and barley, trees such as oak, almond and pistachio expanded significantly. Grassland animal species such as gazelle also expanded, both in range and population. The principal physical changes to the Levant involved the swift rise in sea level, which inundated large portions of the Mediterranean coastal plain,  and the retreat of the Lake Lisan (which became the modern Sea of Gililee and the Dead Sea), which facilitated human and animal movement across the central and lower Jordan Valley.  REFERENCE = Encyclopedia of Prehistory, Vol. 8, P. 306 - 307/ Date taken from The Oriental Institute of the University of Chicago"								"Epipaleolithic (Kebaran,etc.)"	45	dashed	"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = Epipaleolithic (Upper Palaeolithic, Kebaran, Geometric Kebaran) cultures = Small, open air campsites of mobile groups of hunter-gatherers, with occasional stratified rock shelters and cave sites. Occupations commonly contain hearths; other features include rare, lightly constructed shelters and pits. Blade, bladelet, and flake chipped stone assemblages are the main material remains, including points, scrapers, burins, and microliths. These are sometimes accompanied by bone tools, ochre, and shell bead ornaments. Grinding and pounding equipment on limestone and basalt first appear during the Upper Palaeolithic, and increase in quantity and diversity during the course of the Epipaleolithic. Follows the Middle Palaeolithic tradition, overlaps with the Natufian tradition in Levant, and precedes the Aceramic Neolithic tradition.. Location: The Levant and Mesopotamia. Important sites: Abu Hureyra, Abu Noshra, Avdat area (Boqer Tachtit, Boqer, Ein Aqev, Ein Aqev East, Rosh Zin); Ein Gev, El-Kowm (Nadaouiyeh, Umm el Tlel), El-Wad, Eyan (Mallaha), Gebel Maghara (Mushabi, Lagama), Har Harif (Rosh Horesha, Saflulim, Abu Salem, Ramat Harif), Hayonim Cave and Terrace, Kebara Cave, Ksar Akil, Lower Jordan Valley (Fazael, Urkan e-Rubb, Salibiya), Nahal Hadera (Hefziba), Nahal Oren, Neve David, Ohalo II, Qadesh Barnea, Tor Hamar, Wadi Hammeh, Wadi Hasa (Ain Buhira, Thalab al-Buhira, Tor Sadif, Tor al-Tareeq, Ucagizli, Yutil el-Hasa), Wadi Jilat/Azaq (Jilat 6, Uwaynid, Kharaneh IV), Western Negev Dunes (Azariq, Nizzana, Nahal Lavan, Shunera, Nahal Sekher), Yabrud II-III, Zarzi.. ENVIRONMENT = The Epipaleolithic tradition extends across a period of dramatic climatic change. The tradition begins during the last glacial maximum when the Levant and Mesopotamia were much cooler and drier than today. Over time the climate ameliorated, and by ca. 12,000 B.P., a significant warming trend led to almost ideal conditions for human habitation. By ca. 10,800 B.P., the cooler and drier conditions of the Younger Dryas tool hold of the region, creating the diverse mosaic of environments and microenvironments we see today. Rainfall today varies from over 1000mm to less than 50mm annually, and is generally limited to winter months. Summer across the region are relatively hot and dry.  REFERENCE = Encyclopedia of Prehistory, Vol. 8, p. 122 – 123 "								Middle Paleolithic	102	dashed	"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = During this period, people lived in open air campsites and rock shelters. They used tools primarily made of flint, which were smaller than those of the Lower Paleolithic. These tools included points, scrapers, and blades. They show evidence of extensive retouching, showing that the tools were probable used over an extended period of time. They had a diet of wild goat, sheep, boar, cattle, birds, hare, snail, bat, mouse, red deer, and gazelle. REFERENCE = The Oriental Institute of the University of Chicago"								Lower Paleolithic	2502	dashed	"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = During this period, people lived in open air campsites and used large and rough stone axes, as well as pebble and flint tools, which were probabaly made on site, as we can see from cores and waste at the site of Barda Balka. They had a diet of wild cattle, goat, sheep, horse, and turtle, and maybe Indian elephant and rhinoceros (the only evidence of these last two in Mesopotamia was found at the site of Barda Balka). During this time, new cities were built using Greek grid designs, and while art and architecture of this time reflects a Greek influence, it changed relatively little from previous periods. Writing also changed very little, with the Aramaic alphabet and cuneiform still in use alongside Greek. The old Babylonian gods were still worshipped in traditional temples as well. The Suleucids built 2 capitols, called Seleucia-on-the-Tigris (just south of modern Baghdad) and Antioch-on-the-Orontes (in modern day southern Turkey). The culture was a mix of earlier influences, the most important being those of Sumer and Akkad. The spoken language was a dialect of Akkadian, however the written language was a script from Sumer. In Assyria there were 3 fairly autonomous city states: Nineveh, Ashur, and Arbela. Not much is known about Nineveh and Arbela during this period, however we know that Ashur was ruled by a series of vice regents who engaged in many building projects over the centuries, such as temples, palaces, and walls. They also participated in a profitable copper trade with Anatolia. REFERENCE = The Penguin Encyclopedia of Ancient Civilizations, p. 78, 90-92, 101-102"								Ur III	4.1		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = The core of Ur III was divided into 23 city states which were each ruled by a governor appointed by the king. Outside that core, the land to the north and east was divided into over 90 centers which were ruled by military leaders. Outside that, there was no formal control, however relations were strengthened through political marriages and treaties. This system would have required a lot of organization and accounting to make it work. REFERENCE = The Penguin Encyclopedia of Ancient Civilizations, p. 78/ The Oriental Institute of the University of Chicago"								Early Dynastic III Mesopotamia (Sumerian)	4.55		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = Marked by the beginning of documented history. During this time, many of the epic tales of the previous period were recorded and preserved. Settlement of this period followed the three main branches of the Euphrates River, with cities in different regions competing for control of the fertile agricultural lands. It was not until the end of this period that Sumer merged into a single, solid political entity.  REFERENCE = The Penguin Encyclopedia of Ancient Civilizations, p. 77"								Early Dynastic II Mesopotamia (Sumerian)	4.65		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = Also known as Sumer's Heroic Age. This period is marked by its generation of epic and heroic tales. It is also colored by almost constant war-like conditions. The oldest known king, Men-barage-si, ruled from 2630 BC to 2600 BC and features in many heroic tales. REFERENCE = The Penguin Encyclopedia of Ancient Civilizations, p. 76-77"								Early Dynastic I Mesopotamia (Sumerian)	4.9		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = Early Dynastic Period Mesopotamia (Sumerian)/ Ninevite V Period = This period shows marked urbanization, with most of the population living in walled cities which each functioned as a political unit, having their own rulers and patron deities. This period is also characterized by cuneiform writing and plain, wheel-made ceramics with conical bowls. Follows the Jamdat Nasr tradition and precedes the Akkadian tradition. The early Dynastic tradition is typically divided into three major phases: Early Dynastic I, in which the main features of the tradition are established; a transitional period called Early Dynastic II; and Early Dynastic III, in which written records become common and the Sumerian empire becomes well established. The tradition ends with the conquest of Sumer by Sargon of Agade.. LOCATION = The lower Tigris, Euphrates, and Diyala river valles in what is today Iraq. IMPORTANT SITES = Abu Salabikh, al-Ubaid, Girsu (Tello), Lagash (al-Hiba), Nippur, Tell Asmar, Tell Agrab, Ur (Tell al-Muqayyar, Uruk  (Warka).. ENVIRONMENT = Climatic conditions in the Early Dynastic tradition were similar to those today. Winters were mild, while summers were hot and dry. Mean annual temperature was between 20 – 25°C, and mean annual rainfall is only about 200mm. These conditions were stable to about 2300 B.C., when an abrupt climate change, noted in globally distributed lake, marine, and speleotherm cores, reduced precipitation to c. 20 – 30% from today's levels. The threefold dust spike that accompanied this abrupt climate change was not reduced to Holocene background levels until ca. 1900 B.C. The abrupt change was synchronous with 1.) the Akkadian imperial collapse, as well as the collapse of sedentary systems in Anatolia, Palestine, Greece and the Aegean, and Egypt. 2.) Amorite pastoral nomad movements that challenged state political authorities, as did “The Sandcrossers” in Egypt.  . REFERENCE = REFERENCE = Encyclopedia of Prehistory, Vol. 8, p. 112/ The Oriental Institute of the University of Chicago"								Ubaid 4	6.22		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = This period at Eridu is characterized by large temples with the same plan as later Sumerian temples. This entailed a sactuary with an alter at one end, and the temples were on raised platforms with clear buttresses at regular intervals. [Ubaid divisions made equal within Ubaid interval for now.] REFERENCE = Encyclopedia of Prehistory, Vol. 8, p. 372 - 373/ The Oriental Institute of the University of Chicago"								Ubaid 3	6.44		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = During the Ubaid 3 period, the Ubaid tradition expands into northern Mesopotamia, where it first exists beside and eventually replaces sites of the late Halaf  tradition.[Ubaid divisions made equal within Ubaid interval for now.] REFERENCE = Encyclopedia of Prehistory, Vol. 8, p. 372 - 373"								Ubaid 2	6.66		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = By the Ubaid 2 period, there is evidence that Ubaid influence was beginning to spread beyond its original homeland, as indicated by the presence of Ubaid wares along the Arabian coast. At the site of Tell Abada, buildings were characterized by a T-shaped court, with smaller rooms or other T-shaped rooms to either side. This floor plan would be used in later Uruk and Ubaid structures. [Ubaid divisions made equal within Ubaid interval for now.] IMPORTANT SITES = Tell Abada REFERENCE = Encyclopedia of Prehistory, Vol. 8, p. 372 - 373/ The Oriental Institute of the University of Chicago"								Ubaid 1	6.88		0								Ubaid 0	7.1		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = Hard-filed ceramic vessels consisting of buff or red paste and decorated with geometric designs in black or red paint. Other distinctive objects include bent-clay nails, clay sickles, and reptilian headed figurines. Architectural features include temples and tripartite structures in which large, frequently T-shaped hall is flanked by a series of rooms on each side. Follows the Aceramic Neolithic tradition and precedes the Late Chalcolithic Mesopotamia tradition. In Mesopotamia, the Ubaid represents the earliest known culture. In central Mesopotamia and Khuzistan, it follows the Samarran period and precedes the Uruk period. In northern Mesopotamia, it follows the Halaf period, which it replaces. It precedes the late Chalcolithic period, represented by Tepe Gawra, which is closesly related to the Uruk tradition of southern Mesopotamia.  [Ubaid divisions made equal within Ubaid interval for now.]. LOCATION = Iraq and western Iran, in what are referred to as Mesopotamia and Khuzistan, respectively. The earliest known sites extend from southern Mesopotamia and Khuzistan to as far north as the Hamrin basin in east-central Iraq. IMPORTANT SITES = Tell el-'Oueili. ENVIRONMENT = The Ubaid period was characterised by major climatic changes. During the beginning of this period, summers would have been somewhat hotter than today. Winter temperatures would have been colder. The beginning of the Ubaid tradition was also marked by a wet monsoonal climatic regime, with rainfall beginning earlier in the fall and ending later in the spring. By the end of the Ubaid tradition, climatic conditions were well on their way toward modern values, with perhaps slightly higher annual temperatures and greater aridity at the end of the Ubaid than today. In northern Mesopotamia, annual seasonality appears to have been extreme. Summer were intensely arid. During the winter, violent rainstorms resulted in erosion of dry soils, incision of wadis, and possible destruction of settlements.  REFERENCE = Encyclopedia of Prehistory, Vol. 8, p. 372 - 373/ The Oriental Institute of the University of Chicago"																																								Mesopotamia Events	event	150	230/217/234		off	"A large amount of data for this section came from The Metropolitan Museum of Art's online timeline, which can be found at http://www.metmuseum.org/toah/ as well as the Archaeology Dictionary, which can be found at http://www.archaeologywordsmith.com/info.php, and The Penguin Encyclopedia of Ancient Civilizations, edited by Arthur Cotterell."					EVENT												Separate States	0.082										Cyrus the Great	2.55										"Hittite kingdom overwhelmed by the ""Peoples of the Sea"""	3.182		"EVENT DESCRIPTION = The ""Peoples of the Sea"" swept down through Sryia, probably overwhelming the Hittite kingdom along the way. For this accomplishment, his people came to see him as a god-king, and from then on he was called ""King of the Four Quarters"", and his name was usually written with a marker used for a god.
