Mass-produced, but regionally distinct ceramics. Iron and bronze tools. Distinctive cylinder seals, wall plaques, and votive sculptures. Follows the Iranian Bronze Age tradition and precedes the historic Archaemenian empire. LOCATION =  The main area of Iranian Iron Age developed is located in what is known today as the Khuzistan, Luristan, Fars, and southern regions of Iran. Important sites: Chogha Zanbil, Godin Tepe, Haft Tepe, Hasanlu, Malyan, Susa.  ENVIRONMENT/CLIMATE = The area is dominated by the Zagros mountains and the river valleys that carry runoff and alluvium from their slopes. In the south of the region, Khuzisatan is an arid plain dependent on the highlands for metals, stone, and wood; whereas Fars, to the east of Khuzistan, contains numerous fertile valleys and abundant resources. In the north, the environment of Luristan and Kurdistan are varied dramatically by elevation, forming a mosaic of fertile valleys, upland steppes, and forests.  REFERENCE = Encyclopedia of Prehistory, Vol. 8, p. 196. 50yr added to Encyclopedia/ The Penguin Encyclopediaof Ancient Civilizations, p. 147"								Iranian Bronze Age	5		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = The Iranian Bronze Age is characterised by a shift from stone tools and weapons to those made of copper and bronze. This period is also noted for its development and use of a form of pictographic writing called Proto-Elamite, which may be derived from the slightly earlier Sumerian script. The use of cylinder and stamp seals are also diagnostic markers for this time period. Dynamic changes are also noted in pottery styles in the latter part of this period, c. 4400 B.P., from painted pottery to that of a grey or grey-black ceramic ware. Whether or not these dynamic cultural changes can be attributed to local developments or are the result of an important intrusion of new people into an area is still not clear. None of these developments can be traced back to Mesopotamia or other areas previously in contact with the inhabitants of the Iranian Plateau. The Iranian Bronze Age tradition follows the Iranian Chalcolithic tradition and precedes the Iranian Iron Age tradition.  LOCATION = The Iranian plateay in eastern and southeastern Iran. Important sites: Bampur, Tappeh Hesar, Tepe Yahya, Shakr-I Sokhta, Tureng Tepe, Sialk IV. ENVIRONMENT/CLIMATE = There is no strong evidence that the climate of the Bronze Age was much different from the present twenty-first century, although based on pollen analysis, there is some indication that it may have been slightly wetter. In general, the climate of the region is widely diverse, with great variations in temperature and rainfall in different parts of the country. These extremes develop as the plateau is alternately exposed to the dry-hot summer conditions from northwest India and the Siberian winter anticyclonic winds sweeping in from the northeast. The mountain barriers restrict maritime influences to the coastal lowlands, which generate the special humid subtropical weather pattern in the Caspian lowlands, and the hot oppressive Persian Gulf summer climate. Annual rainfall amounts of 40inches occur only in northwest Iran on the Caspian shore. Dry plateau conditions in southeastern Iran, were the annual rainfall amount is less than 5inches, have cause the formation of the great interior salt deserts referred to as the Dasht-i-Kavir and the Dasht-i-Lut. Because of the aridity of this area, there are few streams that flow year round. the Aras, Sefid, Rud, Gorgan, and Atrek Rivers all empty into the Caspian Sea. The Zaindeh River is the principal stream draining the interior plateau region. Lake Urmia in northwestern Iran is the largest of the many salt lakes and marshes in the region.  REFERENCE = Encyclopedia of Prehistory, Vol. 8, p. 156 – 157"								TOP	5.5										Iranian Chalcolithic	7.5		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = Well-fired, grit and vegetable tempered wares that were either slipped or wet-smoothed and burnished. Common painted wares include red painted wares, black-on-buff, and black-on-red fine wares. Decorations commonly consisted of horizontal or vertical bands of lines, zigzags, checkers, and geometrical designs that were filled with diagonal lines or cross hatching. Stylised zoomorphic motifs were also used to decorate ceramic vessels. Vessels bearing polychrome decorations also appear during the Chalcolithic. Differences in the configuration of painted motifs, as well as surface treatment and vessel forms, provide the major basis for defining regional subtraditions. However, there was considerable variation in decoration and vessel forms, even among neighbouring villages throughout the Chalcolithic tradition. Thus, regional subtraditions only reflect broad patterns of similarities between sites and do not necessarily indicate a complete cultural solidarity between villages that made up the traditions. Follows the Neolithic tradition and is succeeded by the Protoliterate traditions. The Iranian Chalcolithic can be subdivided into an Early (7,500 – 6,800 B.P.), Middle (6,800 – 5,900 B.P.), and Late phase (5,900 – 5,500 B.P.) LOCATION = The Zagros and Elburz mountains and associated lowlands and the plateau region of Iran. Important sites: Dalma, Chogha Mish, Seh Gabi, Tal-i-Iblis, Tepe Sabz, Tepe Yahya. ENVIRONMENT/CLIMATE = The Chalcolithic tradition in Iran coincided with the latter part of the Climatic Optimum (10,000 – 6,000 B.P.). Due to greater solar insulation, summers were somewhat hotter and winters colder than today. In neighbouring Mesopotamia, the Chalcolithic tradition was marked by a wet monsoonal climatic regime, with rainfall beginning earlier in the fall and ending later in the spring. Annual seasonality appears to have been extreme, especially in northern Mesopotamia. Summers were intensely arid. During the winter, violent rainstorms resulted in erosion of the dry soils, incision of wasis, and possible destruction of settlements. The extent to which Iran was affected by these climatic events is uncertain, since the mountain-ringed plateau configuration would have sheltered it from these conditions to some extent. The souther plateau region, which is affected by the monsoon system today, would have been affected by these climatic shifts to a greater degree than the interior face of the Zagros mountains. Like today, summers in this part of the plateau were hot, and winters relatively mild, occasionally dropping slightly below freezing. Most precipitation feel during late fall, winter, and early spring. Additionally, torrential downpours were common during late July and August due to the Indian Ocean monsoon system. However, the amount of precipitation was highly variable from place to place and from year to year. Evidence from the Soghun valley on the southeast portion of the plateau indicates that this region experienced somewhat cooler and wetter conditions during the mid 7th millennium B.P. In contrast to the rest of Iran, the region between the Caspian Sea and the Elburz mountains would have experienced greater annual precipitation. The region would have also had milder winters, and warm, relatively humid summers. By the end of the Chalcolithic tradition, modern climatic conditions emerged out of Iran.  REFERENCE = Encyclopedia of Prehistory, Vol. 8, p. 160 – 161"								Iranian Neolithic	11.5		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = Lithic industry largely based on the production of blade-based tools. Ground stone implements include querns, mortars, fully-ground celts, and pierced stones. Other tools include needles, awls, and spatulas made from bone. Handmade, straw-tempered soft war pottery vessels that had been fired at low temperature came into use during the earliest stages of the ceramic Neolithic. These were frequently decorated with red, or occasionally, black or white paint. Common motifs include oblique linear and geometric designs including nested triangles, lozenges, zigzags, and a netlike pattern. Follows the Iranian Mesolithic and is succeeded by the Iranian Chalcolithic. The Neolithic tradition is divided chronologically into an aceramic and a ceramic phase. In the Zagros region and the adjoining lowlands of western Iran, the ceramic phase consists of the Iranian variant of the Hassuna with some Sumerian influences. Although reliable dates are scarce for the earliest Neolithic occupations, the beginning of the aceramic phase of the Neolithic probably began about 11,500 B.P. The ceramic phase began about 8,500 B.P. and ended about 7,500 B.P. LOCATION = The mountains and foothills surrounding the lower lying Iranian plateau. Certain elements of the Iranian Neolithic associated with the region of the Caspian sea and the Elburz highlands extend into southern Azerbaijan and southern Turkmenistan By the last quarter of the eighth millennium B.P, Neolithic communities began to spread beyond the Zagros region into the surrounding lowlands. Important sites: Ali Kosh, Tepe Asiab, Ganj Dareh, Tepe Guran, Hajji Firuz. ENVIRONMENT/CLIMATE = Sediment cores recovered from several lakes in the Zagros mountains indicate that the climate was on its way towards its current state by the end of the preceding Mesolithic tradition. However, the initial stages of the Neolithic tradition were marked by significant climatic fluctuation, in which average temperature at times were about, and at other times were below that of today. By the end of the Neolithic tradition in Iran, the average summer temperature would have been 2 – 4°C higher than today and winter would have been  – 4°C lower. The Neolithic climatic regime is marked by continental conditions resulting in hot. arid summers and cold, moist winters in the mountains and relatively mild winters in the Khuzistan lowlands. The mountain and basin topography of Iran has a significant influence on climate. The movement of moisture-bearing air masses into the interior regions of Iran is impeded by the highlands surrounding the interior plateau. Thus, the most precipitation falls on the exterior flanks of the mountain ranges with the largest amounts occurring in the region of the Caspian Sea. As a result, the Zagros region was, and still is, considerably more arid than the region between the northern flanks of the Elburz mountains and the Caspian Sea.  REFERENCE = Encyclopedia of Prehistory, Vol. 8, p.215 – 216"								Iranian Mesolithic	20		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = Lithic industry characterised by small blades and blade-based tools. Tools include retouched bladlets, backed bladelets, end scrapers, thumbnail scrapers, borers, and notched and denticulated pieces. A small number of burins were also common as they were duting the preceding Baradostian period. During later stages of the Iranian Mesolithic, geometric microliths came into use. Microburins resulting from the segmentation of blades into smaller pieces are present but relatively compared to contemporary Levantine Epipaleolithic assemblages. Other items include needles, awls, and spatulate tools made of bone; as well as small numbers of pestles, mortars, mullers, querns, and grooved stones made from ground stone. Ground stone implements became more abundant over time and were a common aspect of Terminal Mesolithic# material culture. Caves and rock shelters were extensively used for habitation during the Main Phase of the Mesolithic. This phase of the Mesolithic can be divided geographically into at least two, and probably more subdivisions. In the Zagros mountains and flanking lowlands, it is known as the Zarzian. In the Trans-Caspian region it was originally referred to as the Mazandaranian, but more recently called the Trialetian. Round and oval subterranean dwellings made of stone, wattle, and daub came into use during the Terminal Mesolithic. As of yet, this final phase of the Iranian Mesolithic has only been attested in the Zagros mountains. Follows the Southwest Asian Upper Palaeolithic tradition, which consist of a variation of the Aurignacian know in the Zagros mountain region as the Baradostian. The Iranian Mesolithic is succeeded by the Iranian Neolithic tradition.  LOCATION = The mountains and foothills surrounding the lower lying Iranian plateau with some limited settlement on the interior plateau. The region includes the Zagros mountains of eastern and northern Iraq, parts of southern Azerbaijan and southern Turkmenistan around the Caspian sea, and the highlands of the Elburz mountains. Sites culturally related to the Iranian Mesolithic may have extended into parts of southeast Anatolia. Important sites: Ali Tappeh, Belt Cave, Warwasi, Zarzi, Zawi Chemi Shanidar.  ENVIRONMENT/CLIMATE = Very little paleoclimatic data pertaining to the Mesolithic are available for Iran. Almost all of the data have come from the Zagros mountains. The modern climatic regime is marked by continental conditions resulting in hot, arid summers and cold, moist winters. The highland regions which create a rain shadow which blocks the movement of moisture bearing air masses into the interior regions of Iran. Most precipitation falls on the exterior flanks of the mountain ranges, which the largest amounts occurring in the region of the Caspian Sea. This basic pattern was probably in existence during the late Pleistocene of which the Mesolithic is a part, although with greater seasonal extremes. During the Mesolithic, average temperatures were at least 5° - 8°C cooler than today, with drier conditions would have prevailed. At the same time, the cooler temperatures may have resulted in decreased evaporation rates, resulting in higher lake levels. The cooler conditions may have resulted in a snow line that was 650m lower than at present. Based on the available data, the climate during the Mesolithic would have been characterised by a semi-arid to arid regime consisting of short, relatively mild summers, and longer, extremely cold winters in the mountains, and somewhat more temperate conditions in the sheltered valleys and lowlands. The end of the Mesolithic corresponds with the onset of warmer, more humid conditions.  REFERENCE = Encyclopedia of Prehistory, Vol. 8, p. 198 – 199"								Iranian Upper Paleolithic	40																				Persia Lesser Periods	block	150	230/217/234		off	"A large amount of data for this section came from The Metropolitan Museum of Art's online timeline, which can be found at http://www.metmuseum.org/toah/."						TOP	0.492										Aq Quyunlu Dynasty	0.531		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = The name for this dynasty means ""black sheep"" in Turkish. Follows the Middle Arabian Pastoral and precedes the historic period. LOCATION = The Sinai, Levant, and the Euphrates and Tigris basins below the Taurus and Zagros mountains. Important sites: Bir Hima complex (southwest Saudi Arabia), Jabrin oasis (eastern Saudi Arabia), Hagif series (western Oman).  ENVIRONMENT/CLIMATE = The region was subjected to considerable stress due to the reduction of annual moisture over mush of the peninsula beginning in the late third millennium B.C. Some scholars have posited a dramatic climatic catastrophe/retreat. Others proposed a gradual retreat to more mordern conditions. However, we know little about the early second millennium B.C about conditions for the area.  RESOURCE = Encyclopedia of Prehistory, Vol. 8, p.253"								Middle Arabian Pastoral	5.75		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = The distinction between Early Arabian Pastoral and Middle Arabian Pastoral is largely artificial, but can be supported on the basis of a marked reduction in the lithic inventory, accompanied by the concomitant rise of copper and bronze production. The end of the Middle Arabian Pastoral is clear. Follows the Early Arabian Pastoral traditions and precedes the Late Arabian Pastoral tradition. LOCATION = Arabian Peninsula Important sites: These include Bir Resisim in the Negev, Rajajil (and the possible comtemporaneaous Wadi Sirhan sites) in North Saudi Arabia, Jawa in Jordan, and Khirbet el Umbashi/Hebariye in southern Syria. (See the Late Arabian Pastoral entry for important southern Arabian sites.) ENVIRONMENT/CLIMATE = During the early part of the period, conditions described for the Early Arabian Pastoral largely apply. However, during the latter part, in gradual stages, dessication set in as the summer monsoon system weakened and moved southward towards its more present location. Bronze Age societies of the Middle Arabian Pastoral were eventually faced with alternative solutions for remaining in the Arabian peninsula. The percentage of insolation and rainfall (from 300mm to 250mm annually to under 50mm) decreased annually for most of the region during a 200 - 300 year period.  RESOURCE = Encyclopedia of Prehistory, Vol. 8, p. 286"								Early Arabian Pastoral	11		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = These centre on projectile point traditions recognised primarily from Levantine cultural areas and traditions. Northern regional subtraditions include the Epipalaelolithic (Kebaran, Mushabian, Qalhan, Natufian), blade projectile point traditions (Pre-Pottery Neolithic), and the Arabian Bifacial Arrowhead Traditions associated with the Pottery Neolithic. Central and Southern subtraditions are restricted to blade projectile point types and later bifacial variations. For much of the Early Arabian Pastoral, architectural remains may also be important such as kites, stone circle complexes and pillars. Early rock art should also be considered from sites in the Arabian Shield. Follows the Arabian Upper Palaeolithic tradition and precedes the MAP tradition. In terms of regional sequences, the peninsula is divided into the North versus the Centre/South. Northern sequences from the Jordan desert, Syria and Israel are tied into Levantine Epipalaeolithic and Neolithic affiliation. The bulk of the Peninsula has virtually no recognised Epipalaeolithic traditions and the term Neolithic is widely used to cover the period from 8000 – 5750 B.P.  LOCATION = Arabian peninsula. Important sites: Doura Cave and the Kowm basin environs in Syria, the Azraq basin (Kharaneh and Jilat series), the Black Desert (harrah) of eastern Jordon (Kallat Anaza, Dhuweila, Burqu), and the Saudi Arabian sites associated both with the northern and southern margins of the Nafud desert (Kilwa, Umm Wual, Hail, Jubba), Thumama, Saudi Arabian Rub al Kahali lake series, the Ramlat Sabatayn, the Yemeni Saadah locales, Habarut, and the Omani Wadi Ghadun/Hailat Araka locations (Matafa TA 95 – 150).  ENVIRONMENT/CLIMATE = The Early Holocene represents a significant improvement from today's climate, due in part to the northward displacement of the Inter-Tropical Convergence Zone (ITCZ) caused by the Southwest Monsson. Summer rainfall exceeded 250mm on average over much of the peninsula, and created permanent lakes in the Rub al Khali, Nafud, and the desert regions of southwest Iraq, eastern Jordon, and southwest Syria. Average centigrade temperatures were a number of degrees higher. By 5750 B.P. a decline in precipitation had begun, which resulted in modern conditions by 5000 – 4000 B.P.  RESOURCE = Encyclopedia of Prehistory, Vol. 8, p. 77 – 78"																		S. Once they were the protectors of the Holy Cities of Islam, the Ottomans took charge of organizing the yearly pilgramage to Meacca, and ensuring the safety of pilgrims. The Ottoman style of architecture found in Istanbul begins to be found in this region. For more information, visit <a href=""http://www.metmuseum.org/toah/ht/?period=08&region=wap#/Key-Events""> The Metropolitan Museum of Art: Arabian Peninsula, 1400-1600 A.D.</a> or <a href=""http://www.metmuseum.org/toah/hd/otto1/hd_otto1.htm""> The Metropolitan Museum of Art: The Art of the Ottomans before 1600</a>. RESOURCE = MET Timeline"								Mamluk rule	0.742		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = The slave soldiers of the Ayyubid sultans overthrew the last Ayyubid sultan in Egypt, al-Malik al-Ashraf, and established their own rule. Within a short period of time, the Mamluks controlled the greatest Islamic empire of the later Middle Ages. This included control of the Islamic holy cities, Mecca and Medina. The capital, Cairo, became the economic, cultral, and artistic center of the Islamic world. For more information, visit <a href=""http://www.metmuseum.org/toah/hd/maml/hd_maml.htm""> The Metropolitan Museum of Art: The Art of the Mamluk Period (1250-1517)</a>. This period is characterized by well made tanged arrowheads, handmade coarse ceramics, stone vessels, copper and bronze tools, burials typically in mounds or cairns.  Economy based on fishing, hunting, and herding, stone archticture, manufacture of stone/flint tools, stone and shell beads, and other ornaments and small the presence of amounts of Ubaid pottery and other small items from Mesopotamia. Follows the Early Arabian Littoral tradition and precedes the Late Arabian  Littoral tradition.   LOCATION = The east and southeast coast of the Arabian peninsula. Important sites: Barbar (Bahrain), Hili 8 (Oman), Ra's al-Hamra 5 (Oman), Ra's al-Qal'at (Bahrain), Saar (Bahrain), Umm an-Nar (Oman) ENVIRONMENT/CLIMATE = The Arabian peninsula has been arid throughout the Holocene. However, in the middle Holocene a period of higher precipitationled to an expansion of grasslands and a consequent increase in both plant and animal populations. It was at this time that human groups began penetrating the Arabian peninsula and exploiting the ocean and coastal resources that figure prominently in the Early Arabian Littoral tradition. A return to a more contemporary climate took place during the Middle Arabian Littoral tradition. A return to a more contemporary climate took place during the Middle Arabian Littoral tradition. Grasslands were reduced, and settlement was largely restricted to a narrow coastal belt were good agricultural soils, a high water table, and ocean and coastal resources allowed sedentary life to develop. ENVIRONMENT/CLIMATE = The Arabian peninsula was arid throughout the Holocene. However, moderate grasslands were apparently present around lakes that formed in basins adjacent to stabilize dunes. In the Middle Holocene, a period of higher precipitation led to an expansion of these grasslands and a consequent increase in both plant and animal populations. At this time, human groups began penetrating the Arabian peninsula and following a broad spectrum hunting-fishing-gathering lifestyle. Of particular importance to these foragers were ocean and coastal sites figure prominently in the Early Arabian Littoral tradition.   RESOURCE = Encyclopedia of Prehistory, Vol. 8, p. 75"								Arabian Late Paleolithic	40		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = Small, often crude blade tools made of local flints. Stemmed or tanged blade projectile points appear late in the tradition. Follows Mousterian, precedes Early Arabian Littoral and Early Arabian Pastoral. Location: Northern portions of the Arabian peninsula and southern portions of the Near East.  The interior of the Arabian peninsula was, as it is today, hyperarid. Despite its aridity, the area supported a variety of scattered plant covers and grasses. Small lakes created by runoff from stabilised dunes provided regular sources of water for animals and humans. A great diversity of arid-adapted animal species was present, including oryx, gazelle, ostrich and camel. RESOURCE = Encyclopedia of Prehistory, Vol. 8, p.24"																		N. For more information, visit <a href=""http://www.metmuseum.org/toah/ht/?period=02&region=ssa#/Key-Events""> The Metropolitan Museum of Art: South Asia, 8000-2000 B.C.</a> RESOURCE = MET online"								Indus Neolithic	9		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = Semi-sedentary to sedentary communities with permanent buildings made of hand-formed mud brick or pise and structures made with posts and plastered reeds. The main subsistence items are wild grains and wild animals, along with domestic sheep/goat and cattle, and cultivated wheat and barley. Wild fruits such as dates and jujube supplemented the diet. There is a phase of preceramic Neolithic basketry, some with bitumen coating and unfired clay containers. This is followed by the introduction of fragile low-fired pottery, some of which is basket impressed. Other finds include bone and antler tools, blade technology and retouched blades or geometric microliths. There are stone vessels and grinding stones with pestles and a wide variety of ornaments, including locally produced soft stone beads and beads made from exotic materials. Flex burials are located in cemeteries or abandoned domestic areas. Follows the South Asian Upper Palaeolithic tradition and precedes the Early Indus tradition.  LOCATION = The main type site is Mehrgarh on Kachi plan of Baluchistan at the foot of the Bolan Pass. Sites with possible Neolithic occupation levels have been found in other parts of the Indus valley, western piedmont zone, and upland plateaus, sucah as the Quetta valley. Sites can be expected along the Makran coast and delta near Karachi. Important sites: Mehrgarh, Kili Gul Mohammad. ENVIRONMENT/CLIMATE = The greater Indus valley is dominated by two different weather systems that sometimes overlap, the winter cyclonic systems of the western highlands and the summer monsoon system in the peninsular regions. Along the southern Indus river in Sindh, there is often little or no rain, but the rivers bring down the floodwaters from the north and cover the land with rich silts. When both the winter and summer rains are bountiful, the land is extremely rewarding. Usually one or the other system provides enough water to sustain agriculture and herding from one year to the next; it is extremely rare for both systems to fail. The climatic cycle in place today is probably not that much different from that experienced by the Indus Neolithic settlements although evidence suggests a slightly moister climate with a stronger summer monsoon and more season fluctuation of temperatures. In July, it would have been warmer at present and in January slightly cooler.  RESOURCE = Encyclopedia of Prehistory, Vol. 8, p. 151 to 152"																		Indus Valley Lesser Periods	block	150	230/217/234			"A large amount of data for this section came from The Metropolitan Museum of Art's online timeline, which can be found at http://www.metmuseum.org/toah/."						TOP	0										TOP	3.3										Late Harappan	3.8	dashed	"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = This period is characterized by the breakdown of the previously integrated culture of the Indus Valley region into small, localized groups. No styles or forms are shared throughout the various regions, but some general similarities are seen in the use of sandy applique and polychrome decorations. Pottery shapes include globular jars with short necks and simple everted rims, bowls with simple rims and bilaterally projecting (nailhead) rims. Terracotta bangles fired grey with incised designs are found in many sites, and in the later phases red-fired bangles begin to appear. Geometric button seals made from bone, shell terracotta, and fired steatite are found in most sites. Pre-firing potter's marks and post-firing graffiti are also a common feature. By around 4800 B.P., some of the post-firing graffiti can be identified as a form of Early Indus script. At this same time, we see the first use of cubical limestone weights, steatit seals with script, and animal motifs and clay sealings in trade goods. Follows the Indus Neolithic tradition and precedes the Mature Indus tradition. LOCATION = Settlements are found throughout most of modern Pakistan, parts of northwestern India, specifically the states of Haryana, Uttar Pradesh, and Gujarat. There is evidence for direct contanct with the Seistan region of Afghanistan and southeastern Iran, but so far no actual Early Indus sites have been discovered in those areas. Important sites: Harappa, Balakot, Mehgarh/Nausharo, Kalibangan, Kot Diji, Gumla, Sarai Khola, Jalilpur, Rehmandheri, Amri, Ghazi Shah.  ENVIRONMENT/CLIMATE = The greater Indus valley is dominated by two different weather systems that sometimes overlap, the winter cyclonic systems of the western highlands and the summer monsoon system in the peninsular regions. Along the southern Indus river in Sindh, there is often little or no rain, but the rivers bring down the floodwaters from the north and cover the land with rich silts. When both the winter and summer rains are bountiful, the land is extremely rewarding. Usually one or the other system provides enough water to sustain agriculture and herding from one year to the next; it is extremely rare for both systems to fail. The climatic cycle in place today is probably not that much different from that experienced by the Early Indus settlements although evidence suggests a slightly moister climate with a stronger summer monsoon and more season fluctuation of temperatures. In July, it would have been warmer at present and in January slightly cooler.  RESOURCE = Encyclopedia of Prehistory, Vol. 8, p. 116 to 117"								Aceramic Neolithic (Pre-Pottery Neolithic)	10.5		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = Stone or mud-brick architecture; lithic assemblages with high percentage of blade or bladelet production; medium to large symmetrical projectile points; domesticated wheat, barley, peas, and lentils; managed  or domesticated goats; clay animal and human figurines; stone or plaster vessels, but pottery is rare. The Aceramic Neolithic marks the beginning of village life in Southwest Asia. Aceramic Neolithic villages had many attributes of agricultural communities: large settlement size, substantial architecture long settlement duration, intensive  harvesting of seeds with sickles, equipment and facilities for storing and grinding seeds, and containers. Follows the Natufian tradition and is succeeded by the Ceramic Neolithic tradition.  LOCATION = The Levant, Syria, northern Iraq, southeastern Turkey Important sites: Abu Hureyra, 'Ain Ghazal, Basta, Beidha, Bouqras, Göbeckli Tepe, Jarmo, Jericho, Kfar ha-Horesh, Mureybet, Nahal Hemar, Nemrik 9, Netiv Hagdud, Qermez Dere, Tell Maghzaliyah ENVIRONMENT/CLIMATE = The period began during the cold, dry Younger Dryas. About 10,000 B.P. , an increase in temperature and humidity left the lowlands frost-free in winter. Today, in most parts of Southwest Asia, seasonal precipitation is concentrated from November to April. There is some evidence that Holocene summers had rain, possibly because of a northerly route of monsoon winds. In general, the Aceramic Neolithic experienced relatively favourable conditions.  RESOURCE = Encyclopedia of Prehistory, Vol. 8, p.1"								South Asian Late Paleolithic	30		"INTERVAL CHARACTERISTICS = A comparatively recenty recognised cultural stage, Upper Palaeolithic tradition of South Asia awaits good habitational deposits and rich cultural remains. The blade based toolkit of the major tradition is marked by mediocre blade technology -1/3 to 50% of the blades in a collection, in which the fine long blades are restricted. Various forms of backed blades and knives are fairly well made. Burin technology is not common and when found exhibits a few simple forms. Microblades and microliths form an intergral part of the collections. There is also little evidence for art and craft activities and use of other media. however, in some regions, bone, ostrich eggshells, and marine shell objects have been found. Follows the East Asian Middle Palaeolithic tradition and the South Asian Microlithic tradition.  LOCATION = This tradition seems to exist in small regional pockets, the important ones being the Vindhyan hills and the Ganga plains of Central India, the Tapi valley of Maharashatra, and the mideastern coastal region falling in Andhra Pradesh. Some less prominent groups appear to exist in parts of the West Bengal and Thar desert of Western India. Important sites: Ainchwara, Baghor (Son Valley), Laharia-dih, Chopani-mando, Sarai-Nahar-Rai, and Patne. ENVIRONMENT/CLIMATE = At the technocultural change from the Middle Palaeolithic to the Upper Palaeolithic, climatic conditions of South Asia were generally tropical and humid. But the development of blade based culture evidenced arid and hot conditions. The paleoclimate studies carried out in the Thar desert of western India indicate that short climatic cycles of dry and humid conditions prevailed in the peninsula during the Terminal Pleistocene and Early Holocene periods. Particularly at around 18,000 B.P., climatic conditions were marked by lower precipitation. This was the time when blade-based cultures flourished on a large scale, and in comparison to early Upper Palaeolithic times, had a wider spread. Sites of the earlier phase, the Middle Palaeolithic, were many, in camparison to which Upper Palaeolithic localities were restricted. However, in the succeeding phase, around 18,000 B.P., the Upper Palaeolithic mode of subsistence gained momentum. The following wet phase of around c. 10,000 B.P. gave impetus to the Mesolithic way of life, which in archaeological records is the transition stage between Upper Palaeolithic and the early Mesolithic. It may be further assumed that Upper Paleolithic tradition flourished only in regions of South Asia where the climatic conditions during the Terminal Pleistocene and early Holocene periods were moderate and the fluctuations were short cycles of humid and dry spells. The cold regions such as the Himalayan mountains of north India and wet zones of the eastern Indian subcontinent remain devoid of Upper Palaeolithic and Mesolithic remains.  RESOURCE = Encyclopedia of Prehistory, Vol. 8, p. 326 to 327"																		Indus Valley Events	event		210/190/200	nititle	off	"A large amount of data for this section came from The Metropolitan Museum of Art's online timeline, which can be found at http://www.metmuseum.org/toah/."					FAD												Pakistan independence	0.053										Wadi Suq period	3.7		"EVENT DESCRIPTION = The focus shifts back to the Arabian mainland and a new style of pottery and different tombs are found; some cities such as Tell Abraq continue in use, but there are some major changes in settlement patterns RESOURCE = Encyclopedia of Prehistory, Vol. 8, p. 284"								Localization era begins	3.9										Early Dilmun (Barbar)	4.2		"EVENT DESCRIPTION = The focus shifted to Bahrain, which became a flourishing trading post in the copper trade between the Indus, Oman, and Mesopotamia. Implements less than  1-5 cm long were used as composite tools and are easy to identify. They were made from locally available fine grained rocks and occasionally, silicified and fossilised wood. The types of microlithic tools found are blades, points, arrowheads, burins, borers, awls, scrapers, small choppers, and many others. Other finds are ground plans of circular and oval huts, hearths, charred wild grains, fragile handmade pottery, anvils and hammer stones, animal bones, antlers and shells, human burials (both single and double) with grave goods, bone ornaments and artifacts, pieces of hematite and ochre pieces and a number of painted rocks shelters. Follows the South Asian Upper Palaeolithic tradition, precedes and overlaps with the later Neolithic traditions of South Asia."								TOP	12000		Top of the South Asian Upper Paleolithic 								South Asian Upper Paleolithic	40000		"Tools made using retouched blades, including scrapers, burins, backed knives, and projectile points. A microlithic technology develops and becomes predominant late in the tradition. The settlements were small and aphemeral, representing seasonal base camps for some ten or twenty people. Primary locations were in caves or large rock shelters with easy access to water and where several environments (valley floor and uplands) could be exploited. Most were forested areas or in locations with ready access to firewood. Follows the Mousterian tradition, precedes the Iranian Mesolithic tradition. Other material attributes include bone/antler artifacts, Neolithic axes and adzes of basalt and granite, microliths of chert, silica, chalcedony, agate, and carnelian. Microliths include parallel blades, blunted-back blades, points, scrapers, lunates, triangles, trapezes and tranchets. Large stone objects include food-processing equipment like quern, muller, and rubber, and miscellaneous objects like stone balls. The introduction of iron did not have much effect on the overall lifestyle. Follows the South Asian Microlithic tradition and precedes the Gangetic India tradition. The dates from the Mahagara and Kunjhun II suggest the beginning of the western Neolithic tradition around the mid-fifth millennium  B.P. - some 500 years before the eastern Neolithic. Source: MET Timeline. Follows the Mature Indus tradition and precedes the historic period. The Indian subcontinent emerges from prehistory during the Vedic period, beginning at about 1000 B.C. the earliest Sanskrit texts date to this era or possibly a century or two earlier. Unfortunately, final site reports have yet to appear for the important sites of Eran, Nagda, and Ujiain, making it difficult to assess chronology and characteristics of the period. Black and red ware is widespread. Other wares of the same period are more localized and include micaceous red wares and black slipped wares, among others. Ornaments of semiprecious stones, coral, ivory, glass, terracotta, and agate beads. In the Vidarbha region, megalithic mortuary features occur in small and large cemeteries; all are stone circles with internal stone cairns. Follows the Central Indian Chalcolithic tradition (c. 4700 – 3100 B.P.) and precedes, and in some areas overlaps with, the historic period. "								Central Indian Chalcolithic	5		"Three distinct Chalcolithic cultural traditions, namely Kayatha, Ahar, and Malwa, have been identified and are commonly referred to as the Chalcolithic cultures. Culturally the various Chalcolithic phases display certain common traits including painted wheelmade ceramics, a specialised blade/flake industry of copper, farming, stock-raising, pastoral and a limited hunting-based subsistence. Constructed either rectangular or round mud houses. Follows the South Asian Microlithic tradition and precedes the Central Indian Iron Age. The recent excavations at Balthal in Rajasthan suggested that the Chalcolithic communities of the Ahar tradition began earlier at about 5000 B.P. whereas the Malwa plateau shows the beginnings around 4200 B.P. This suggests that agriculture and a settled way of life were accepted first in the western part of Central India and then moved eastward onto the Malwa plateau. "																		South Indian	block	150	230/217/234		off							TOP	0										South Indian Iron Age	3.1		"Megalithic mortuary features in a wide variety of forms. These include above-ground and subterranean stone chambers, sometimes enclosed thin stone circles or covered by cairns; rock-cut chambers, urn burials (sometimes with caps stones), stone alignments, and isolated standing stones, among others. Cemetaries vary widely in size. The majority of the reported sites have consisted of less than ten graves, but some have had up to 1500 graves. Diagnostic artifacts include polished black and red ware ceramics; during the latter part of the period, rouletted ware, and russet coated painted wares occur. Copper implements and iron tools and weapons are common, as is evidence of iron smelting. Settlement hierarchy has been documented in several regions, with large settlements ranging from 20-50 ha in size. Follows the South Indian Neolithic tradition (c. 4,700-3,100 B.P), and precedes (and in some areas, overlaps with) the historic period. "								South Indian Chalcolithic	5		"South Indian Chalcolithic cultures are divided on a regular basis on their characteristic ceramic types and paintings thereon and the presence of copper/bronze objects. Their distinctinctive housing types, agropastoral mode of subsistence, and chiefdom-based social structure are also diagnostic. Follows the South Asian Microlithic tradition and precedes the South Indian Iron Age."																		Greater India	event		210/190/200	notitle							FAD												India independence	0.053		"Freedom at Midnight, 14 Aug 1947"								Mauryan Empire	2.323		Peak under emperor Ashoka (286-231 BC) who spread Buddhism. Tool kits are variable and include choppers, scrappers, picks, awls, and gravers, as well as other types. Tools show unifacial and some bifacial flaking, with unidirectional and alternate retouch. Sites are associated with Middle to Late Pleistocene animals, many of which are now extinct. Cultural materials other than stone tools include some evidence of bone modification and controlled use of fire. Bronze and iron working was well developed, and there are some diagnostic items, which include curved bronze daggers and stag and ram's head designs. Despite these features, the primary diagnostic attributes come from burials. The Early Nomads buried their dead in coffins made of stone slabs. In the east, these were large and often surrounded by a circular enclosure, whereas in the west they were sometimes covered by a mound of earth. "								Late Xiajiadian	3.6		"Bronze artefacts such as knives, daggers, and decorative plaques are the best known late Xiajiadian objects. Li and ding tripods and pedestal-leg dou vessels dominate the ceramic assembly. The firing temperatureof these vessels was probably low, producing soft, crumbly ceramics with a nonhomogenuous colour. Investment in permanent domestic structures is usually minimal. More energy is invested in the construction of graves, which are usually earthen pits lined with stone slabs. "								Early Xiajiadian (Xiajiadian Xiaceng)	4.2		"Grey-brown sand-tempered pottery, often decorated with incised net motifs, band decorations, cord marks, as well as application of clay bands and buttons. Gray burnished pottery is less common, while polychrome pottery is confined to graves. Ceramic tripods are common; many are polished. Circular houses, sometimes with two concentric walls, built of stone or stamped earth walls and contain internal platforms and large rectangular structures. Deep pit graves, some with internal wooden structure. "																													Mongolia events	event		208/207/215	notitle	off						FAD												Genghis Khan	0.794		"1206 Temujin is enthroned as the Genghis (""great"") Khan of the Mongols. (http://history.cultural-china.com/en/51History601.html).  Shared stylistic attributes over a wide area suggest increased inter-regional interaction by the late Neolithic period. There is a variety of pottery forms, with the majority dark grey or black in colour. Common decoration techniques are polishing and cord mark impressions. Finely made vessels in burials become extremely thin walled. Ground stone and chipped stone tools for agriculture and wood working appear similar to those from earlier periods. Symbolic jade forms in burials continue to be important. Traces of copper or bronze at a few sites indicate the beginnings of metallurgy. At most sites, houses are circular, built at ground level, and have walls of wattle-and-daub.  Adobe brick and hang tu (or rammed earth) begin to see use in this period. There is evidence for settlement hierarchies in more than one area. Follows the Yangshao tradition in the central Huang He or Yellow River valley, the Dawenkou tradition in the lower Yellow river valley, the Daxi tradition in the central Yangzi river valley. It precedes the Shang tradition the historic period."								Southeast China Late Neolithic	5.5		"Well developed stamped geometric patterns on ceramics. Double-shouldered stone tools. In some areas, large polished stone shoves buried in pits without associated material. Generally small sites and houses. Follows the Southeast China Early Neolithic tradition, precedes the historic period. "								Dawenkou	6.2		"A variety of pottery forms, especially in burials, such as tall stemmed cups, serving stands, tripod cooking pots, tripod pitchers, and jars. Decorative techniques include polishing, engraving open work design on stems, and embellishment of form (thin walls, tall stems). A few painted vessels have been found, primarily from the early phase. Ground stone agricultural and woodworking tools, jade ornaments, and symbolic jade tools (apparently unused) in burials. Rectangular and circular houses (mainly ground level, some subterranean), primarily wattle-and-daub. There is little information on settlement patterns. Follows the Peiligang tradition, precedes the Longshan tradition. "								"Peilingang, Yangshao, Hongshan, Daxi, Majiabang"	8.5	dashed	"Materials commonly found on Peiligang sites include red and brown slipped handmade ceramics fired at low temperature; a diversity of ground stone tools, including axes, hoes, serrated scythes, and distinctive  four footed mortars; microlithic blade tools; and a variety of bone tools, including arrows and harpoons armed with bone points. Follows the Ordosian tradition, precedes the Yangshao and Dawenkou traditions."								Southeast China Early Neolithic (Dapenkeng)	9	dashed	"Thick, grit tempered, cord-marked ceramics, often with incised geometric designs around the rim. Pebble choppers and flake knives and scrapers. Ground stone adzes, axes, scythes, hoes, and grinding slabs. Follows the Ordosian tradition, precedes the Southeast China Late Neolithic tradition."								Ordosian	40	dashed	"Small stone tools made from flakes by hard hammer direct percussion predominate. Tool kits are variable in composition and include simple flakes, scrapes, notches, denticules, points, borers, backed knives, burins, and bone tools. From about 20,000 B.P., blades are an important component, and mircolithic technology is well established throughout the region by 10,000 B.P. Other than cultural remains from caves and rock shelters, hunter gatherers of this tradition are not associated with substantial Paleolithic dwellings.  Encompasses the Upper Paleolithic and the Pleistocene/Holocene transition. Follows the East Asian Middle Paleolithic tradition and precedes the Peiligang tradition. More than one chronometric date is reported for the Ordosian sites. Most of these dates were obtained more than 20 years ago before new quantitative techniques were refined to the point they are today. Because it is now possible to date some loess sediments, the chronology of the Chinese Paleolithic in this region is currently in a dynamic state. "																													North China	block	150	208/217/215		off							TOP	0										Republic	0.088		"A republic evolves amidst warlordism and conflicts between the Nationalist and the Communist parties. Settlements desplay plans with public courtyard, dwelling area, and graveyard. Storage pits are filled with greyish cultural deposit. Follows the Peiligang and precedes the Longshan tradition."								TOP	8.5	dashed	Top of the Ordosian								Ordosian	40	dashed	"Small stone tools made from flakes by hard hammer direct percussion predominate. Follows the Jamon tradition and continues into the historic period. "								Jomon (Final)	3										Jomon (Late)	3.5										Jomon (IMiddle)	4.5										Jomon (Early)	7										Jomon (Initial) 	10										Jomon (Incipient) 	12	dashed	"The term ""Jomon"" (cord mask) is derived from typical pottery decoration of this period. This term is used both as the name of the time period and culture as well as of the pottery used in this period. Typical materials found on Jomon sites include Jomon pottery , clay figurines, clay masks, pendants, antler harpoons, and spears. "								Japanese Late Paleolithic (Sendokian)	20		"Blade debitage cores, Setouchi technique cores, burins on truncation with retouched base (Araya type), burins on inverse truncation (Kamiyama type), backed points with retouched base, flake points with retouched blade. "																													Southeast Asia - Mainland	block	150	208/217/215		off							TOP	1.5		"Top of the Island Southeast Asia Late Prehistoric (Indo-Malasyaian Archipelago Early Metal Phase, Sa Huynh-Kalanay) and Mainland Southeast Asia Late Prehistoric"								Mainland Southeast Asia Late Prehistoric	2.5		"This period is considered the interface between the late prehistoric and early historic periods and is sometimes called the Protohistoric period. Complex polities emerged during this time along the coasts and in the river valleys of mainland Southeast Asia, and it is also likely that many subsistence strategies like hunting and gathering continued into this period. At its early end (2500 – 1500 B.P.), some archaeologists have also called this period the “Iron Age” “General Period C” and the “High Metal Age”. The period after A.D. 200 falls into Higham's “General Period D” and at the beginning of the regionally specific chronologies. In central Burma, we see the initiation of the Pyu period, and in central Thailand we see the origins of the Dvaravati period. In the Khorat Plateau/Tonle Sap region, the founding of the Khmer empire signals the end of this period in A.D. 802. In Vietnam, this is a transitional phase between Bronze Age complex societies and the earliest states. The period from 2500 – 1500 is glossed over as “the early historic period”.    Encyclopedia of Prehistory, Vol. 3 p. 160-162   "								TOP	4		Top of the Hoabinhian								Hoabinhian	10		"Definition of the Hoabinhian assemblages included unifacially flaked pebble tools, particularly ""sumatraliths"" an elongated oval pebble with flakes removed from one of its faces, and a short axe, which made of flat and oval pebbles of flakes, flaked only on one face, and broken into into halves. Along with diagnostic unifacial pebble and flake tools, Hoabinhian assemblages also included unifacial flaked tools, edge-ground stones, large flakes, untilised flakes, a large amount of debitages, and cord-marked ceramics.  However, bone and shell tools are reported from a number of Hoabinhian sites. Settlement patterns include cave, rock shelters, and shell middens. Follows the Southeast Asia Upper Palaeolithic tradition and precedes the Southeast Asia Neolithic and Bronze Age tradition. "								Southeast Asia Upper Palaeolithic	40		"Between 40,000 and 30,000 B.P., unifacial pebble tools and utilised flakes (generally of scraper-like morphology when retouched), as well as bone tools of various types, seem to characterised most Southeast Asian assemblages. On the Asian mainland, there is a predominance of tools made from volcanic and metamorphic river pebbles. In the islands, there was much greater availability of findgrained rocks such as chert. Some regions lacked good stone, and the resulting industries are essentially without regular form. Core preparation for elongated flakes is claimed before 17,000 B.P., there is an industry of elongated backed flakes, microliths, and hollow based projectile points dating to the mid-Holocene after 700 B.P. The open site of Tingkayu (possibly 28,000 – 17,000 B.P.) in Sabah has an industry, so far unique in Southeast Asia, of chert bifaces. Less standardised bifacial working in chert was also present more than 25,000 years ago in rock shelters in Southern Thailand. In general Southeast Asia in this period has a lack of stone spear points or knives and an overall lack of tools demonstrating purposeful thinning and retouch flaking. Prismatic blade technology appears only in Neolithic, uniquely so far at the site of Bukit Tengkorak in Sabah, were it dates to 3500 B.P. Nevertheless, two technological innovations of considerable significance were made during the Late Pleistocene. These are the edge grinding of pebble axe, reported widely but of variable date in Vietnam, Peninsular Malaysia ans Sarawak (the oldest ones could date to 30,000 years ago as in Japan and Australia), and the manufacture of large shell adzes, reported from about 13,000 B.P. in the northern Moluccas. Follows the East Asian Middle Palaeolithic tradition, precedes the Hoabinhian tradition on the Southeast Asian Mainland and northern Sumatra and the Island Southeast Asian Neolithic tradition in the islands."								TOP	200		Top of the Zhoukoudian								Zhoukoudian	600		"Locality 1 has produced a sequence of deposits 40m thick, rich in both artefacts and fossils. The fossil remains of more than 40 individuals attributed to Homo erectus include 14 partial crania, as well as many teeth and postcranial elements. Artefacts are made of vein quartz and consist mostly of flakes. Anvils and bipolar flakes and cores were fashioned by retouching along edges of quartz chunks or flakes produced primarily scraper forms, as well as forms classified as choppers, points, and awls. First tradition in East Asia; precedes the East Asian Middle Palaeolithic "																		Korea sub-interval	block	150	208/217/215		off							TOP	2.5		Top of the Mumun								Mumun	4		"Coarse pottery with little or no surface decoration, flat based, handmade, and low-fired. Polished stone implements. Bronze daggers of Machurian or Liaoning type. Megalithic structures (dolmens), stone cists, and jar burials. Semisubterranean houses with rectangular, square, and circular floor plans often in large groups. Follows the Chulmun tradition, precedes the historic period. In Korean literatures, generally referred to as the Bronze Age of Korea. "								"Chulumn (Pissalmun, Comb-Pattern)"	8		"Coiled pottery jars with wide mouths, decorated on the exterior with incising, impressing, appliqué, or pinching. Chipped and ground stone tools often made of slate. Subrectangular, semi-subterranean houses with a square central hearth. Houses are grouped in small villages. which tend to cluster on streams, coasts, and islands. Sites on the coast are often associated with shell mounds. Follows the Ordosian tradition and precedes the Mumun tradition. "																		Indo-Malaysia	block	150	208/217/215		off							TOP	0										"Island Southeast Asia Late Prehistoric (Indo-Malasyaian Archipelago Early Metal Phase, Sa Huynh-Kalanay)"	2.5		"Decorated pottery, predominately incised, assigned to the Sa Huynh-Kalanay tradition. Large and small jars, bowls, boxes, plates, and cups, with high necked vases as a distinctive widespread form that transcended local traditions. Ceremonial bronzes with geometrical and stylized figureative motifs, including gongs (called bells and kettle drums in literature), axes, and flasks. Glass beads of the Indonesian Pacific variety, plus semiprecious stone beads imported from India. Boats made out of sewn planks lashed directly to the frame. Wooden housing on piles can be assumed despite spotty support from the archaeological evidence. Follows the Southeast Asian Neolithic and Early Bronze, precedes historic."								Southeast Asia Neolithic and Bronze	6.5		"Generally characterised by pottery and polished stone tools, with some “Bronze Age” qualities, with some continuing “Mesolithic” flaked stone tools industries in remote regions. The region is so vast that a simple summary is not possible. Bronze artefacts during this period were restricted to northerly parts of the mainland of Southeast Asia and made an initial appearance c. 3500 B.P. Technologies everywhere reflected adaptation to tropical conditions, particularly in the use of organic materials such as wood and bamboo. Follows the South-east Asia Upper Palaeolithic and Hoabinhian traditions, precedes the South east Asia Late Prehistoric traditions Many hunter-gatherer economies continues in isolated regions during this period, especially in Island South-east Asia. This period also encompassed the primary dispersal of two major agriculturalist  populations: speakers of the Austroasiatic languages on the Mainland South-east Asia, and the speakers of the Austronesian languages in the Philippine and Indonesian islands"																		Australia intervals	block	150	197/217/234		off							TOP	0.2		Top of the Late Australian								Late Australian	7		"Backed artefacts, points, tulas, axes (in the south), millstones, earth mounds, shell mounds, stone structures, complex polychrome art, and high density cemeteries. Follows Early Australian, precedes the historic period. "								Early Australian	40		Precedes Late Austrian																													Australia event	event		197/217/234		off						FAD												Australian Core Tool and Scraper Tradition	32		Lake Mungo sites (from Archeology Dictionary site)																		Oceania - Micronesia	block	150	208/217/215		off							TOP	0.2										Micronesian	3		"All prehistoric human material fall into the Micronesian tradition, as these were the first human inhabitants of the islands."								Lapita	3.5		"The Lapita peoples lived on the islands of Melanesia and Western Polynesia, where they favoured the coastal interface between land and sea, building their settlements on either beach or terraces or on stilt houses situated over reef flats or shallow lagoons.  The Lapita people extensively exploited the rich marine resources of reefs, lagoons and open sea as a primary source of food, while they used the jungle for its rich plant life and for planting gardens.  The oldest radiocarbon dates thus far obtained come from sites in the Bismarck archipelago, especially the Mussau islands, and are about 3500  - 3400 calibrated B.P. These earliest sites constitute what is sometimes called the “Early Far Western” subtradition of major expansion of Lapita. Between about 3200-2900 B.P., there was a Western region (Bismarck and main Solomons), into Remote Oceania. No later than 2900 – 2800 B.P., Lapita had reached its maximum geographic distribution, with the most easterly known sites in the Manu'a islands of Samoa, and the most southerly on the New Caledonia. Terminal dates for the Lapita tradition vary in different localities. In the Bismarck archipelago, distinctively new styles of ceramic decoration commence by around 2700 – 2300 B.P., although there is probably cultural continuity. In the Western Polynesian region, the distinctive dentate-stamped ceramic style does not persist for more than two or three centuries, giving way to variants of Polynesian plainware by 2500 B.P. "																		Oceania - Polynesia	block	150	208/217/215		off							TOP	0.4		Top of the Rapanui or Rapa Nui								Rapanui or Rapa Nui	1.5		"Stylised monolithic stone sculpture (moai) in human form associated with distinctive ceremonial architecture (ahu). Elliptical houses (hare paenga) of pole and thatch with dressed curbstone foundations and associated beach cobble pavements. Flat obsidian spearheads (mata'a) of various sizes and some named shapes, each with percussion-flaked tang, and one-piece fishhooks (mangai) of bone and stone.  "								Polynesian expansion	2		"Lapita ceramics are replaced by Polynesian plainware ceramics, which in turn disappear but are not replaced. Thus the presences of ceramics is a key material indicator of the early part of the tradition and their absence of the later part. Monumental burial and substructure mounds and earthwork fortifications are also diagnostic of the later part of this tradition. "								Somoan	2.5		"Lapita ceramics are replaced by Polynesian plainware ceramics, which in turn disappear but are not replaced. Thus the presences of ceramics is a key material indicator of the early part of the tradition and their absence of the later part. Ground stone adzes, flaked stone tools, and shell tools are also characteristic of the tradition. Follows the Lapita colonization of the Samoan islands and contines into the historic period."																		Oceania - Melanesia	block	150	208/217/215		off							TOP	0.9		Top of the Fijian								Fijian	2.4		"Impressed ceramics until roughly 900B.P., incised ceramics thereafter. Stone adze blades and other stone tools. Earthwork fortifications are a major feature of the landscape in many parts of Fiji. Follows the initial Lapita colonisation of the Fiji islands, continues into the historic period. "								Melanesian	2.5		"The absence of Lapita pottery is a prime diagnostic feature of the Melanesian tradition, and pottery of the Melanesian tradition itself is both rare and diverse , so that it does not form a good diagnostic category. Large, well made stone axes or adzes have been found, but most other tools made of bone, shell or wood have not been preserved. Obsidian blades were used in some areas. Follows the Early Australian and Lapita traditions, precedes the historic  period. "								Lapita	3.5		"The Lapita peoples lived on the islands of Melanesia and Western Polynesia, where they favoured the coastal interface between land and sea, building their settlements on either beach or terraces or on stilt houses situated over reef flats or shallow lagoons.  The Lapita people extensively exploited the rich marine resources of reefs, lagoons and open sea as a primary source of food, while they used the jungle for its rich plant life and for planting gardens.  The oldest radiocarbon dates thus far obtained come from sites in the Bismarck archipelago, especially the Mussau islands, and are about 3500  - 3400 calibrated B.P. These earliest sites constitute what is sometimes called the “Early Far Western” subtradition of major expansion of Lapita. Between about 3200-2900 B.P., there was a Western region (Bismarck and main Solomons), into Remote Oceania. No later than 2900 – 2800 B.P., Lapita had reached its maximum geographic distribution, with the most easterly known sites in the Manu'a islands of Samoa, and the most southerly on the New Caledonia. Terminal dates for the Lapita tradition vary in different localities. In the Bismarck archipelago, distinctively new styles of ceramic decoration commence by around 2700 – 2300 B.P., although there is probably cultural continuity. In the Western Polynesian region, the distinctive dentate-stamped ceramic style does not persist for more than two or three centuries, giving way to variants of Polynesian plainware by 2500 B.P. "								Mumum (New Guinea)	4		"Coarse pottery with little or no surface decoration, flat based, handmade, and low-fired. Polished stone implements. Bronze daggers of Machurian or Liaoning type. Megalithic structures (dolmens), stone cists, and jar burials. Semisubterranean houses with rectangular, square, and circular floor plans often in large groups. Follows the Chulmun tradition, precedes the historic period. In Korean literatures, generally referred to as the Bronze Age of Korea. "								New Guinea Neolithic	10		"The New Guinea Neolithic peoples were the first food producers and had the first sedentary communities in New Guinea; thus, major diagnostic features are the presence of agricultural fields and sedentary communities. Crude flaked stone tools, including retouched flakes, bifaces, and pebble tools become an important feature of the tool kit over time. Follows the Early Australian tradition, precedes the historic period. It should be noted that this entry focuses on the highlands, which were occupied for at least 25,000 years.  The coastal areas show little occupation until roughly 4,000 B.P. and demonstrate some cultural features quite different from the highlands. For coastal areas, readers should consult the Melanesian entry. "																		Oceania migrations	event		197/217/234		off						FAD												Hawaiian	0.8		"Begins with the initial colonisation of the Hawaiian islands and precedes European contact in A.D. 1778 Bone and shell fishhooks, dry-laid stone temple mounds. Transition between hunter-gatherer and development of farming and pottery.  Source = Archeology dictionary.								Gravettian Culture	30		"An Upper Paleolithic industry named after the site of La Gravette in Southwest France. It is characterized by blade tools called Gravette points (small, pointed blades with straight blunted backs) and female figurines made of ivory. This industry succeeded the Aurignacian and preceeded the Solutrian, c. 28,000-20,000BP. It appears to have developed in central Europe, eventually expanding east and west. The Gravettians invented the bow and arrow, blunted-back knives, and tanged arrowheads. Other common artifacts belonging to the Gravettians are bone and ivory spears, as well as bone tools incised with elaborate geometric patterns."								Late Paleolithic	40		Stone and flint tools (with heavy marginal retouch).  Named after Aurignac site in S. France.								Mousterian  (Homo neanderthalensis)	180		"Archeology dictionary (but WebChron says only 100,000 BP for onset).  Lasted until about 30,000 BP. Also called the Middle Paleolithic. The Mousterian industry was an advancement over the Levallois technique. It used a disc shaped core from which flakes could be struck and then reworked into tools. More flakes could be produced from a single core than in previous industries. (Physical Anth Book)"																		Southeast (Balkans-Ukraine)	:	Southeast (Balkans-Ukraine) - eastern 	Southeast (Balkans-Ukraine) - southeastern 	Southeast (Balkans-Ukraine) -  northern																		Southeast (Balkans-Ukraine) - eastern 	block	150	246/187/202		off							TOP	0.547		Fall of Constantipole 1453 AD = end of Byzantine Empire. Source = MET Timeline								Byzantium	1.676		Constantine comes to power in AD 324. Source = MET Timeline								Roman Iron Age	2.1										TOP	4		"Top of the Eurasian Steppe Nomad (Yamnaya, Katacombnaya)"								"Eurasian Steppe Nomad (Yamnaya, Katacombnaya)"	6.5		"The construction of kurgan mounds over the burial; the construction of burial grounds with stone foundations and cromlechs. The dead were buried individually in quadrangular pits roofed with wooden floors; the position of the dead is supine in contracted posture with the legs raised; the orientation is predominantly eastern; various uses of ochre in the burial rite. The funeral offerings: eggshaped and pointed bottom vessels with a short neck, ornamented with single and double cord impressions, comb impressions; bronze four faceted awls and leaf-shaped knives made of North Caucasus metal; upper quern stones and millstones; pendants made of animal fangs, bone hammer-shaped pins; wooden wagons; anthropomorphic stelae in the western region. Short-term camp sites in the east and permanent settlements in the west. Follows the Eastern European Mesolithic tradition and precedes the Scythian tradition. "								 Eastern European Mesolithic (Epipaleolithic)	11		"The technique of chipping knife-shaped blades, including microblades, was highly developed. Microblades had a variety of geometrical shapes, such as segmented, trapezoidal, rectangular, and parallelogram forms. Microburins were used for producing microliths. Wood-working tools were widely used and included axes, adzes, chisels, and macrolithic specialised tools such as mattocks, picks and hoes. Polishing of stone tools, drilling and sawing were also employed. Bone and antler tools were harpoons, arrows, darts, daggers, knives, spears, axes, adzes and sockets for them. Follows the Cyesar Paleolithic and precedes many of the Neolithic traditions in Central and Eastern Europe. "								Magdalenian	18		"A  distinctive set of typologically defined tools, based on prepared core blade production, including numerous blacked bladelets, dihedra burins, burins on truncation, end-scrapers on blades, percoirs, and becs. An elaborate bone tool technology, including beveled sagaie bone points, single and double row barbed harpoons, and eyed needles. Production of mobiliary art on utilitarian and non-utilitarian objects, including animal, human and geometric motifs, and parietal art, including many of the celebrated painted caves of Southwestern France. Top of the Northern Mediterranean Mesolithic (Epipaleolithic), in some places to c.6500 B.P. Last tradition of the European Upper Paleolithic, follows the Solutrean tradition, precedes the Mesolithic tradition of Europe."								Aurignacian	40		"Stone and flint tools (with heavy marginal retouch).  Named after Aurignac site in S. France. The Aurignacian people are the first modern humans in Europe. In addition to the anatomical differences from the earlier Neandertal (Mousterian) peoples, the Aurignacians brought with them a distinct stone tool technology based on the use of blades struck from blade cores. The also produced some of the first works of art. Follows the Mousterian tradition and precedes the Perigordian tradition."																		Southeast (Balkans-Ukraine) - southeastern 	block	150	246/187/202		off							TOP	0.547		Fall of Constantipole 1453 AD = end of Byzantine Empire. Source = MET Timeline								Roman Iron Age	2.1										East-cental European Iron Age	2.8		"Scythian people displaced Cimmerian; and then expanded into Anatolia and Iran, etc.  Source = Archeology dictionary Distinctive early pottery styles; wheel-thrown wars are late. Iron weapontry, with swords of the ""akinakes"" type. Figural toreutic are in the Thracian animal style. Defended settlements; monumental stone architecture on urban sites; upland sanctuaries. Imported Greek and Persian luxury goods. Follows the Western European Late Bronze Age and Northeastern European Bronze Age, partially overlaps with the the Northeastern European Iron Age. Precedes the historic. "								TOP	3.1		"Top of the Southeastern European Bronze Age (Cycladic, Cypriot, Helladic, including Myceanean, Minoan periods)"								Southeastern European Bronze Age	5.1		"Cycladic, Cypriot, Helladic, including Myceanean, Minoan periods.  The main diagnostic features of this age are the ceramics, which can be used to dilineate regional subtraditions as well as periods and phases within the Southeastern European Bronze Age. Ceramics are usually well made, and may be slipped and/or burnished, painted, incised, or impressed with patterns. Minoan hieroglyphics, Linear A (undeciphered), and the Mycenaean Linear B script are present at some sites. Bronze weapons and ornaments are also present and further serve as cultural and chronological markers. Follows the Northern Mediterranean Neolithic Age, and preceeds the historic periods beginning with the Dark Age and continuring from there. It is divided into Early, Middle, and Late Bronze periods. (E.B.A. c. 5,100 – 4,000B.P.; M.B.A. c. 4,000 – 3,500B.P.; L.B.A.. c. 3,500 – 3,100B.P.) "								Southeastern European Late Chalcolithic	5.7		"The tradition is characterised by the emergence of massive copper tools and by prestige items made of gold, the development of surface architechure, and the gradual complexity of form and growth in size of settlements. The East Balkan area was characterised by fortified nucleated settlements of tell type with necropoles outside the residential area, and the eastern Carpathian-North Pontic area by flat macrosettlements with central plazas. Specific for the tradition was waddle-and-daub house with wooden and clay platform. Medium and large sized flint blades or massive stone axes are also characteristic. Ceramic vases very well fired, were decorated with angular or/and curvilinear patterns wiith graphite or incision and encrusted colour in the east Balkan and north Danube zone. In the eastern Carpathian area, bi- or thrichrome painting was practised. Anthropomorphic and zoomorphic clay figurines and architectural clay models are also diagnostic features of the tradition. The eastern Carpatian and northwestern Pontic area is characterised by the absence of necropoles. Follows the Southeastern European Early Chalcolithic tradition, precedes the Southeastern European Bronze Age. "								Southeastern European Early Chalcolithic	6.2		"The southeastern Europe Early Chalcolithic tradition is characterised by incipient use of copper, the emergence of surface architechture, houses in rows and circles, and the beginning of fortified tell settlements. Ceramics ranked into two classes (for common use and prestige) are the diagnostic features of the tradition. Presitge ceramic vases decorated with angular or/and curvilinear incised patterns. crusted ware with red and white and with graphite towards the end of the tradition, as well as  anthropomorphic, zoomorphic and architechtual clay figurines and models are also diagnostic features. Stone instruments as medium-sized flint blades or stone axes are characteristic for the whole Balken-East Pontic area. Small copper instruments, rolled copper beads made by hammering. Crouched and extended inhumation, first necropoles separated from settlements, specialsed cultic areas. Follows the Southeastern Europe Neolithic and precedes the Southeastern Europe Late Chalcolithic tradition. "								"Southeastern European Neolithic (Karanovo, Sesklo) "	8		"The first evidence of plant and animal domestication and the first ceramics in the region. Ceramic begins as plain wares in simple bowl and jar forms but later are slipped and painted with geometric designs and have more diverse and complex forms. Ceramics are well-known and are the subject of detailed typologies and analyses. Polished stone axes first appear and stone grinders, bown awls, and shell beads are also diagnostic of this tradition. The chipped stone industry consists primarily of blades or bladelets. Anthropomorphic and geomorphic figurines become common. Follows the Northern Mediterranean Mesolithic and precedes the Southern European Chalcolithic."								Solutrean	22		"Projectile points.  Named from Solutré, site in central France."								Perigordian (Upper Perigordian)	30		"Lithic technology of the Perigordian includes Gravette and micro-Gravette points, tanged Font-Robert points, truncated elements, and Raysse and Noailles burins. Although the diagnostic types pertaining to the Peridordian V subphases were originally considered to be chronologically segregated, recent excavations at the southwestern French site of Le Flageolet I has demonstrated that the tools (specifically Font-Rober points, truncated elements, and Noailles burins) may coexist in the same Perigordian V archaeological unit. Antler, bone and ivory implements include Isturitz sagaies; a range of long slender sagaies (projectile points), some with a groove; sagaies whose base has a single side-bevel (Gravettian lateral-bevelled points); sagaies with roughly cut or hacked bases; and short, bipointed sagaies with round section (similar to the Aurignacian spindle-shaped points) or flat section. Artistic expression takes the form of painting, engraving, and bas relief descriptions on cave walls along with the production of three-dimensional sculpted forms, most notably female figurines and personal ornaments. Settlement patterns most likely include seasonal use of home-base and special-activity camps in rock shelters, caves and the open air. Follows the Aurignacian tradition and precedes the Solutrean. "																		Southeast (Balkans-Ukraine) -  northern	block	150	246/187/202		off							TOP	0.547		Fall of Constantipole 1453 AD = end of Byzantine Empire. Source = MET Timeline								Holy Roman Empire	1.038										Romano-Celtic (Proto-Celtic)	2.1		"Introduction of Roman artefacts and architecture. Artefacts include Roman coinage (gradual disappearance of coinage of local rulers), terra sigillata pottery, post-Dressel 1 amphorae, Roman-style jewellery; features include rectilinear buildings and fortresses, use of stone and mortar, Roman-style temples and villas, baths, amphitheaters, paved roads. There is also a type of non-Roman temple architecture that occurs mainly later in the period, with small squarish stone buildings, probably two stories, surrounded by a portico. Follows West-Central European Late Iron Age, precedes the historic period. "								Celtic (La Tène) culture (West Central European Iron Age)	2.4		"Characterized by iron tools, particularly knives and swords. Diverse handmade and wheelmade pottery and unique styles of decorative art. Villages of neatly arranged wattle-and-daub houses sat next to large cemeteries containing either inhumations or cremations. Weapons were usually buried with men and jewellery with women. Follows the West-Central European Early Iron Age tradition and precedes the Romano-British and Roman Iron Age traditions. By about 2500 B.P., the techniques to forge iron tools and jewellery made of low grade steel were widespread in Europe, and remained largely unchanged until the Middle Ages. The Celtic expansion starting about 2480 B.P., commonly referred to as the La Tene phase of Celtic culture, marked the division between the Early and Late Iron Age in Europe. The end of the prehistoric Iron Age was heralded by the Roman conquest in the Alpine zone as far as the Danube River (c. 2015 B.P.) "								West-Central European Early Iron Age (Hallastatt C/D)	2.8		"Extensive use of iron and bronze for personal ornament, tools, and weapons; geometric design on pottery, which begins to be made on the fast potters wheel this period. Trade in amber, glass, tin, graphite, and imported luxury goods from the Mediterranean and the East Alpine region. Mortuary ritual includes inhumation and cremation under tumuli as well as in flat graves cemeteries. Appearance of high status  tumulus burials known as ""Fustengraber"" or ""princely burials"" in the West Hallstat area.  Settlement patterns includes dispersed hamlets. as well as hill forts of 3-50 ha often located on or near major river systems. Hallstatt period, follows the Late Bronze Age/Unifield period, precedes the Late Iron Age/La Tene period."								TOP	4		Top of the European Early Bronze Age								European Early Bronze Age	4.7		"Appearance of bronze tools and onaments and local bronze manufacture; appearance of new metal types, such as elaborate shaft-hole axes and the first blade weapons (swords); continuation of copper tools and ornaments. Outstanding features include formation of tell-sites in Pannonia and other lowland areas; fortified sites in the surrounding uplands; shell tempered pottery; separate cemetery sites; a great deal of regional diversity in material cultures (i.e., ceramics);  and new modes of subsistence. Cultural traditions become increasingly regional and localised. The Early Bronze Age (EBA) follows the European Eneolithic and precedes the Northeastern and the Western European Bronze Age traditions. "								Hallstatt cultures	6		"Pottery decorated with twisted cord ornament found in north and central Europe. Until ca 2450 BP (site). Associated with Battle-Ax culture, which may have been an influx of Indo-European language group. Source = Archeology dictionary."								Linear Pottery culture (Linearbandkeramik (LBK) culture)	6.5		Ceramics decorated with incised or impressed lines or bands; shoe-last adzes; and long houses. Follows the Western European Mesolithic tradition and precedes the Middle Neolithic period. 								Magdalenian	18		"A  distinctive set of typologically defined tools, based on prepared core blade production, including numerous blacked bladelets, dihedra burins, burins on truncation, end-scrapers on blades, percoirs, and becs. Production of mobiliary art on utilitarian and non-utilitarian objects, including animal, human and geometric motifs, and parietal art, including many of the celebrated painted caves of Southwestern France. Last tradition of the European Upper Paleolithic, follows the Solutrean tradition, precedes the Mesolithic tradition of Europe."																		S France-Iberia (West Central)	block	150	246/187/202		off							TOP	0.208		French Revolution in 1792.
