Brought copper metallurgy to north and west Europe. Source = Archeology dictionary. The Bell Beaker is a characteristic type of beaker with a form similar to an inverted bell. It is usually finely decorated with horizontal bands made with incised, impressed, or excised techniques, and it is usually ornamented all over. The vessels are excellently made, well finished, and thoroughly fired to a red-brown or reddish colour. Associated with the Bell Beaker is a group of objects called the Bell Beaker ""package"". Among these objects there are tanged copper daggers, ornaments of sheet gold, tanged flint arrowheads, V-perforated buttons, wristbands to protect the archer's forearms, and ornaments such as bracelets in the shape of a half moon. Chalcolithic and in some areas, until the beginning of the Early Bronze Age. "								Corded Ware culture	6		"Pottery decorated with twisted cord ornament found in north and central Europe.  Associated with Battle-Ax culture, which may have been an influx of Indo-European language group. Source = Archeology dictionary."								Impressed Ware	6.8		"Impressed Ware pottery, polished stone artefacts, transverse arrowheads. Follows the Western European Mesolithic, and is commonly referred to as the Early Neolithic period."								Azilian	12.5		"Most dramatically is the disappearance of Upper Paleolithic parietal art tradition. Azilian art consists of smooth pebbles painted, and occasionally engraved, with red and black lines, dots, and borders. Less common designs include simple geometric elements such as crosses and chevrons. The Azilian appears sometime after about 12,500 B.P. during the Bolling Interstadial oscillation and fades after 9,500 B.P. with the end of the Younger Dryas. "								TOP	16.5		Top of the Solutrean								Magdalenian	18		"A  distinctive set of typologically defined tools, based on prepared core blade production, including numerous blacked bladelets, dihedra burins, burins on truncation, end-scrapers on blades, percoirs, and becs. An elaborate bone tool technology, including beveled sagaie bone points, single and double row barbed harpoons, and eyed needles. Production of mobiliary art on utilitarian and non-utilitarian objects, including animal, human and geometric motifs, and parietal art, including many of the celebrated painted caves of Southwestern France. Last tradition of the European Upper Paleolithic, follows the Solutrean tradition, precedes the Mesolithic tradition of Europe."								Solutrean	21		"Standard Upper Paleolithic lithic stone tools endscrapers, burins, perforators, sometimes backed blades and bladelets, associated with bifacially or unifacially invasively flaked points and knives “laurel” and “willow leaves”, invasively flaked or marginally backed shouldered points, and more rarely, tanged points. Bone artefacts include antler points, eyed bone needles, and rare spearthrowers, the latter two types invented in the Solutrean. Follows the Gravettian (Upper Perigordian), precedes the early Magdalenian (Badegoulian)."								TOP	25		Top of the Aurignacian								Perigordian	30		"Flint industry. Early Perigordian is not found in upward continuity with Late Perigordian which was an Upper Palaeolithic industry characterized by well-developed blade tools of flint and female figurines of ivory. Succeeded the Aurignacian and preceded the Solutrean, c 28,000-20,000BP. The small, pointed blades with straight blunted backs are called Gravette points. Most of the French sites are caves, but possibly related industries, known as Eastern Gravettian, are distributed through the loess lands of central Europe and Russia at the camp sites of mammoth-hunters. The Gravettians invented the bow and arrow, blunted-back knives of flint, and the tanged arrowheads. They are famous for their cave paintings. Other artifacts include bone or ivory spears and, in eastern Europe, numerous other bone tools incised with an elaborate geometric pattern."								Aurignacian	40		"Stone and flint tools (with heavy marginal retouch).  Named after Aurignac site in S. France. The Aurignacian people are the first modern humans in Europe. In addition to the anatomical differences from the earlier Neandertal (Mousterian) peoples, the Aurignacians brought with them a distinct stone tool technology based on the use of blades struck from blade cores. The also produced some of the first works of art. Follows the Mousterian tradition and precedes the Perigordian tradition. "																		Western/West-Central Europe	block	150	246/187/202		off							Top	1.518		482 AD = begin Merovingian; 409 AD = begin Visigoths. Source = MET Timeline								Roman Iron Age	2.033		"Three-aisled long houses with wooden and wattle-and-daub walls or turf walls in some areas, often oriented west-east, with entrances in the long sides and often a byre area. Handmade pottery, extensive use of Roman imports. Use of urned cremation or furnished inhumation. Offerings of animals, humans, weapons, and other material in watery areas. Follows the West-Central European Late Iron Age, precedes the historic period."								West-Central European Late Iron Age	2.7		"Distinctive early pottery styles; wheel-thrown wares are late. Iron weaponry, with swords of “akinakes” type. Figural toreutic art in the Thracian animal style. Defended settlements; monumental stone architecture on urban sites; upland sanctuaries. Imported Greek and Persian luxury goods. Follows the Western European Late Bronze Age and Northeastern Bronze Age, partially overlaps with the Northeastern European Iron Age. Precedes the historic period. Because iron is in regular use from at least Hallstatt A2 period (3050 B.P.), scholars in Bulgaria and Romania choose to date the beginning of the Iron Age early. However, characteristically “Hallstatt” social and economic forms do not emerge until Hallstatt C; thus, conventional, pan-European terminology is retained here. The early Iron Age Hallstatt phases (Ha C and D) are contemporary with the Orientalizing, Archaic, and early Classical periods of the Greek Black sea colonies; the later, La Tene, Iron Age phases (LT A-D) correspond to the later Classical and Hellenistic periods, and are ended by Roman conquest. "								Western European Late Bronze Age (Urnfield) 	3.3		"Use of bronze for most tools, weapons, armor, elite vessels, and jewelry, with iron used for small objects and decoration on bronze objects. Distinctive handmade burnished and slipped pottery, often decorated with channelling, fluting, incising, bosses, and graphite. Geometric and solar symbolism. Cremation burial in urn graves in communal cemetaries often adjacent to contemporary settlements. Introduction of new foods such as millet, oats, rye, and flax. Domestic horses become more common.  Dynamic system of trade. Ideology of warrior elite, expressed in arms and armor, symbolism, settlement pattern, and burial rites. Follows the western European Middle Bronze Ae tradition and precedes the west-central European Early Iron Age tradition."								Western European Earlier Bronze Age (Tumulus Culture)	3.8		"Two major changes take place in the earlier Bronze Age to differentiate it from the preceding Bell Beaker and earlier traditions is 1.) the use of bronze, primarily for weapons and ornaments; 2.) the burial in single graves (i.e. noncommunal), and in many areas under a small mound of earth; and 3.) the construction of fortified settlements, particularly in central Europe. Follows the Bell Beaker and European Bronze Age traditions. "								Linear Pottery culture (Linearbandkeramik (LBK) culture)	6.5		Ceramics decorated with incised or impressed lines or bands; shoe-last adzes; and long houses. Follows the Western European Mesolithic tradition and precedes the Middle Neolithic period. 								Western European Mesolithic	9.5		"Characterized by distinctive microliths as well as by flint axes, adzes, and picks made fully or partially using the new technique of stone grinding. The people of this time also made spears, harpoons, mattocks, and other types of tools out of deer and elk bone and antler, as well as using wood for dugout canoes, axe gandles, and spear shafts. Sites are usually small and found on lakeshores. Thus sites are abundant in fish bones and fishing equipment such as fish hooks, basket traps, and nets. The mesolithic begins later in northwestern Europe. The Maglemose or Maglemosian tradition appears in southern Scandinavia sometime after 9,500 B.P., spreads to adjacent regions of the European plain, and persists until c. 7,500 or 7,000 B.P."																		British Isles - NW France 	block	150	246/187/202		off							TOP	0										"Tudor, Stuart, Hanoverian, Windsor"	0.515										Plantagenet	0.846										Norman	0.934		Norman conquest of Britain in 1066. Follows the Impressed Ware and Linear Ware traditions, precedes the Corded Ware, Bell Beaker, and Early European Bronze Age traditions. "								Western European Mesolithic	9.5		"Characterised by distinctive microlithic stone repertoire as well as by flint axes, adzes and picks made either entirely by flaking or in combination with the new technique of stone grinding. Maglemosians also made spears, harpoons, mattocks, and other types of tools of deer and elk bone and antler and made wide use of wood for dugout canoes, axe handles, and spear shafts. The wooden bow and microlith tipped arrow appear to have been the main weapon. Sites have been small and located along lakeshores. Sites are abundant in fish bones, fishhooks, basket traps, and seine nets, leisters, and other fishing equipment. The Mesolithic begins later in northwestern Europe. The Maglemose or Maglemosian tradition appears in southern Scandinavia sometime after 9,500 B.P., spreads to adjacent regions of the European plain, and persists until c. 7,500 or 7,000 B.P. Elaborate development of bronze and iron weaponry. Decorative and iconographic art styles. Evidence for social hierarchies with marked ethnic, and gender differentiation in funerary evidence. Bronze was used for a variety of items, mainly weapons and personal ornaments. Burials were often in small mounds, and both inhumation and cremation were practised. Follows the Corded Ware tradition, precedes the Northeasten European Iron Age tradition."								"Corded Ware culture (Battle Axe, Globular Amphorae)"	6		"Key diagnostic artifacts are ceramics decorated with impressed cord markings, usually in the form of parallel lines around the vessels neck. Ceramics are relatively crude, often flat bottomed, and with beaker forms common. In some areas globular amphorae with rounded bottoms, stone axes with a large hole for hafting (commonly called” battle axes”) are also diagnostic artifacts for the tradition. Some have argued that in addition to these artifacts, flexed burials with the battle axes and beakers, and demonstrating sexual differences in body placement, are also diagnostic of the Corded Ware tradition. Follows the Eastern European Mesolithic and Western European Mesolithic traditions. Precedes the Northeastern European Later Bronze age. Many scholars argue that the Corded Ware tradition represents early members of the Indo-European language group. "								Linear Pottery culture (Linearbandkeramik (LBK) culture)	6.5		Ceramics decorated with incised or impressed lines or bands; shoe-last adzes; and long houses. Settlement sites in Transcaucasia are relatively small (1-2 ha.) to medium sized ( c. 12 ha.) until Late Bronze times (c. 3500-3000 B.P.) when they can be considerably larger; settlements in the northern Caucasus are less numerous and smaller. Both areas are characterised by rich raised burial mounds (kurgans) from late Early Bronze times on (c. 4300 B.P.). This tradition is one of the leading Old World metallurgical centers from late Early Bronze times on - producing weapons, tools, and jewellery made frist of arsenical, then of tin-bronzes, as well as rich silver and goldwork, inlaid with semiprecious stones. Follows the Caucasian Neolithic and Chalocolithic traditions, precedes the historic period in the region. Bronze weapons and tools, decorative artefacts made from copper and gold, stone arrowheads, axes, and maces. Large rectangular half-dugout dwellings of frame/post construction made from wood, sometimes stone bases or clay daubing. Specific funeral rites: graves, consisting of dirt burial mounds or usually a round or rectangular stone wall, sometimes with a burial mound embankment at the center. The burial is in a dirt gravel pit, often with a wooden lining, a stone box, or a cyst for ritual inhumation and cremation. Accompanying the burial may be a horse, ox, or ram, vessels, decorations, and occasionally knives, weapons, or chariots. Follows the Kelteminar and Yamnaya traditions and precedes the Scythian tradition. "								 Eurasian Steppe Nomad	6.5		"The construction of kurgan mounds over the burial; the construction of flat burial grounds with stone foundations and cromlechs. The dead were buried individually in quadrangular pits roofed with wooden floors; the position of the dead is supine in contracted posture with the legs raised; the orientation is predominantly eastern; various uses of ochre in the burial rite. Funeral offerings eggshaped and pointed bottom vessels with a short neck, ornamented with single and double cord impressions, comb impressions; bronze four-faceted awls and leaf-shaped knives made of North Caucasus metal; upper quern stones and millstones; pendants made of animal fangs, bone hammer-shaped pins; wooden wagons; anthropomorphic stelae in the western region. Short term camp sites in the east and permanent settlements in the west. Follows the Eastern European Mesolithic tradition and precedes the Scythian tradition. "																		Caucasian steppes to South Siberia - West Central Asia	block	150	246/187/202		off							TOP	0										Russian empire	0.5		1550 AD is an average for Russian dominance. Ceramics are crude, often globular in form and with rounded bases. Flaked stone arrow points are present, and projectile points, knives, and other stone tools made of bone are common. Late in the period, horse and cattle appear along with their obvious accoutrements such as saddles and muzzles.  "								Siberian Neolithic and Bronze	8		"Microliths inset into bone and antler tools, bone/antler harpoons, polished lug (trunnion) adzes, polished slate points and knives, and comb-stamped ceramics are the major diagnostic cultural remains of the Neolithic. Copper tools begin to appear in small numbers about 4,000 B.P. Bronze itself is very rare and restricted to the far southwestern portion of the region; hence the term ""Bronze Age"" is something of a misnomer. "								TOP	10		Siberian Late Upper Paleolithic								Siberian Late Upper Paleolithic	19		"Wedge-shaped core and microblade technology, slotted antler and bone poiints, transverse burins, smalll end scrapers, and large side scrapers are the major diagnostic cultural remains of the Late Upper Palaeolithic. Diagnostic hominid fossils are anatomically modern. "								Siberian Middle Upper Paleolithic	25		"Parallel and subprismatic core and blade technology, small end scrapers, canted gravers, ivory female statuettes and other works of mobiliary art, and substantial semisubterranean dwelling features are the major diagnostic cultural remains of the Siberian Middle Upper Palaeolithic. Diagnostic hominid fossils are anatomically modern. "								Siberian Early Upper Paleolithic	42		"Parallel and subprismatic core and blade technology, unifacial points on blades, angle burins on blades, and end scrapers on blades are the major diagnostic cultural remains of the Siberian Early Upper Palaeolithic. Diagnostic hominid fossils are absent. "																		Baikal	block	150	208/217/234		off							TOP	3	TOP of the Baikal Neolithic and Bronze									Baikal Neolithic and Bronze	8	"Lithic blade industry, including mircoblade technology. Ground slate fishhooks shanks, shaft straighteners, adzes, knives, and ornaments (the last three also have nephrite). Bone/antler leister prongs, harpoon heads, and ornaments. Small, thick-walled, decorated ceramic vessels of various shapes. Inhumation cemeteries, some with evidence of cremation. In the later part of the period, addition of copper alloy fishhooks, needles, knives, and ornaments. "																			Amur	block	150	208/217/234		off							TOP	1.5		Top of the Amur Neolithic and Bronze								Amur Neolithic and Bronze	12		"Flat bottomed ceramic vessels are characteristic vessels are characteristic  of the Amur Neolithic. These are decorated in a variety of styles, including stamping, cord marking, and textile impressing. Incised horizontal zigzag designs are characteristic during much of the period. Well-made lanceolate projectile points with slightly excurvate bade edges and a deep basal notch are also diagnostic. Follows the Amur Paleolithic, precedes the historic period in the region. "								Amur Paleolithic	30		"Amur Palaeolithic assemblages are defined by primarily being nonceramic. There are some distinctive laurel-leaf shaped projectile points and knives associated with the Amur Palaeolithic, as well as crude pebble choppers and scrapers. Tehre is some use of Levallois-type disk cores and a few examples of prismatic blade cores. Follows the East Asian Middle Palaeolithc tradition, precedes the Amur Neolithic tradition. "																		Northeast Asia	block	150	208/217/234		off							TOP	3		TOP of the Holocene Stone Age of Northeast Asia								Holocene Stone Age of Northeast Asia	10.5		"Very significant cultural changes took place in northeast Asia around the Pleistocene-Holocene boundary. Two of the most characteristic featuresof the local Palaeolithic tradition became extinct: the wedge-shaped core method of flint knapping, which was replaced by the microprismatic technique; and bifaces, which remained out of use for the next several millennia until Neolithic times. The appearance of microprismatic technology is for the most important technological step. Microprismatic technology is present, with frequent modifications, in all local cultures of the Holocene Stone Age. The beginning of Neolithic is marked by the appearance of bifaces and the appearance of ceramics. Follows the Siberian Palaeolithic tradition and precedes the Siberian Protohistoric tradition. "																		Central Kamchatka	block	150	208/217/234		off							TOP	0.5		TOP of the Old Itel'men								Old Itel'men	5		"Diagnostic stone tools  including scrapers, perforators, knives, including polished ones, polished axes, burins. Bone tools such as harpoons, including toggling, composite, and whole-cut fishing hooks, net needles, scrapers, and arrow points. The presence of toggling harpoons implies the use of boats to hunt sea mammals in the open sea. Fishing nets, hooks, spears, and wiers are common. Ceramics of the Ainu-type (Neiji) and Koryak type are found. Nettle-fibre textiles are present. Houses are of two forms: light summer dwellings made of branches that were probably covered with skins, and semisubterranean, rectangular sod houses. Follows the Tarya Neolithic tradition, precedes the historic period. It is the direct ancestor of the historically known Itel'man people. "																		Kamchatka	block	150	208/217/234		off							TOP	2.5		TOP of the Tarya Neolithic								Tarya Neolithic	4		"Stone bifacially worked tools and polished axes, including flat retouched leaf-shaped, triangular, and stemmed points; scrapers on flakes and blades; leaf-shaped and stemmed backed knives; polished and chipped axes of triangluar and oval shape. Fishing gear, including net sinkers and hooks. Ceramics were made, although they are not well known. Textiles were made from woven nettles. Light, frame summer houses and semi-subterranean winter houses. Follows the Kamchatka Mesolithic tradition and precedes the Old Itel'men tradition."								Kamchatka Mesolithic	8		"Stemmed points on blades; bifacial tools; conic, prismatic, and pencil cores; mircoblades; ceramic are rare or non-existent. Follows the Siberian Late Upper Palaeolithic, precedes the historic."																		Alaska - North	block	150	208/217/234		off							TOP	0.25	Dashed	Top of the Aleutian								TOP	2.5		Western Arctic Small Tool								Western Arctic Small Tool	4.7		"Numerous relatively narrow microblades; bipointed projectile points and sides blades suggest the presence of the bow and arrow. Indented base points possibly used harpoon inserts, delicate burins, and well fashioned scrapers of chipped chalcedony, all noted for small size and delicacy of workmanship. Houses are known from only a few sites located at rivers and lakes in the interior portions of the occupation ranges; these are usually roughly square, 3 - 5m on a side, and excavated into the contemporary surface up to .5m. Temporary campsites are much more widespread, a small minority of them at coastal locations. Follows the Northern Archaic tradition, with terminal portions of which it coexisted; precedes the Norton tradition, a modified descendant with portions of which remnant Western Arctic Small Tool assemblages coexisted. Some researchers in North Alaska expand the Western Aractic Small Tool tradtition to also include the Norton tradition. "								Aleutian	6		"Diverse lithic and bone tool industry. Stone tools are mostly unifacially or bifacially retouched and include projectile points, knives, scrapers, and adzes. Other stone tools include pecked stone oil lamps, fishing sinkers, and ground stone knives. Bone tools include wedges, unilaterally and bilaterally barbed bone spear and harpoon points, and bird-bone needles and awls. Follows the Paleo-Arctic tradition; precedes the historic period of the enthnographically known Aleut culture. "																		Alaska - East	block	150	208/217/234		off							TOP	0										"Eskimo, Athapaskan"	0.4		"Arbitrarily placed in ""historic"" (after 1600)"								TOP	2.7		"Eastern Arctic Small Tool, and Kodiak"								Eastern Arctic Small Tool	4		"Tent rings, including midpassage structures; spalled burins and microblades; both tang-basd, barbed, nontoggling harpoon heads and open-socketed toggling harpoon heads; triangular  and bipointed end blades for harpoons and arrows. Represents the earliest human occupation of the region; precedes Dorset. "																		Alaska - South	block	150	208/217/234		off							TOP	1		TOP of the Norton								Norton	3		"Harpoon, arrow, and dart heads of antler or ivory; labrets; chipped projectile points and side blades; settlements chiefly on the coast of major rivers, campsites in the interior. Distinctive art style in one subtradition (Ipiutak). Cermanics - generally fibre tempered, linear, or check stamped - as well as stone lamps and minor polished slate in two subtraditions (Choris, Norton). Burins in one subtradition (Choris). Follows the Western Arctic Small Tool tradition, althought probably coexistent with final aspects of it; precedes the Thule tradition, although contemporary for some centuries with early regional aspects of it. Some investigators working in North Alaska incorporate this tradition in an expanded Western Arctic Small Tool tradition. "								Kodiak (Kachemak)	4		"Small, single-room houses with depressed floors, clay-lined interiors pits, rectangular stone slab hearths, and slab-covered floor drains; artefacts from human bones and other ritual treatment of and dismemberment of the dead; ground slate main lithic technology in late phase; abundant notched pebble net weights, grooved cobble line weights, cobble spall tools, stone lamps, lamps of anthropomorphic and zoomorphic sculptures in late phase; toggle and other harpoon heads, spear prongs, labrets and other ornaments, delicate eyed needles; preservation favours recovery of stone and bone. Follows the Ocean Bay tradition, precedes the historic period."								Ocean Bay	8		"Red-ocher covered floors, large ochre grinders, undefined semisubterranean house (Early), circular semisubterranean house (Late), rectangular stone slab hearths, macroblades (Early) slotted bone points for microblade insets, microblade cores (Early), tiny to large flaked chert projectile points in various styles (including knives), flaked adze bits with only minor grinding, barbed bone harpoon heads in one style with line guards but no line hole (absence of toggling harpoon heads), delicate eye needles, fishhooks,  stone lamps, atlatl hook, distinctive sawn, scraped and ground slate technology that partially replaces flaked stone for projectile points and pointed knives (Late), cobble spall saws, and scapers for slate (Late). Initial in the local sequence, precedes the Kodiak (Kachemak) tradition. Early Ocean Bay arose within or was a part of the Anagula-Chaluka transistion phase (7,800 - 4,000 B.P. uncalibrated carbon-14 years) of the eastern Aleutian islands. "								Paleo-Arctic	11		"Microblades, small, wedge-shaped prismatic cores, disk bifaces, leaf-shaped bifaces, burins. Precedes the Northern Archaic tradition. "																		Alaska - West	block	150	208/217/234		off							TOP	0.1		TOP of the Thule								Thule	2.1		"Culture complex expanded eastward out of Alaska to Greenland beginning by 1000 B.P., established across all the North American Arctic zone by 700 B.P.  Toggling harpoon heads, drag floats for harpoon lines, sealing darts used with a throwing board, bird darts, bolas, fish spears. Single and double holed, enclosed men's boats (kayak) and open multipassanger women's boats (umiak) were used in hunting and travel. Ground stone replaced chipped stone tools throughout the tradition. Pottery in early phases is corrugated or paddle impressed with fibre/sand temper, later a plain ware, thick, less well fires, larger vessel shapes, gravel tempered with incised or punctate designs. Decorative styles of encraving, particularly on ivory harpoon heads, progressed from a complex interplay of animal forms (Okvik/Old Bering Sea) to geometric designs (Punuk/Thule). Pit houses were square to rectangular with entry passage and cold trap. House-pit villages were located on beach strands or headlands adjacent to route of whale migrations. Follows the Norton cultural tradition and merges into the historic period."																		Northwest and Canada - Eastern Subarctic	block	150	208/217/234		off							TOP	0										"Inuit, Athapaskan"	0.4		"Arbitrarily placed in ""historic"" (after 1600)"								TOP	4		Top of the Northern and Shield Archaic								Shield Archaic	6		"Shield Archaic sites are characterised by three primary types of flaked stone tools: scrapers, bifacial knives, and projectile points (often side-notched). Lancolate spear points, knives, fishhooks, and other tools of raw hammered copper are found in areas near copper sources, particularly north of Lake Superior. There is a lack of the ground stone tools generally found in Archaic sites farther south. Follows the Late Palaeo-Indian tradition, precedes the initial Shield Woodland tradition."								Paleo-Arctic	11		"Microblades, small, wedge-shaped prismatic cores, disk bifaces, leaf-shaped bifaces, burins. Precedes the Northern Archaic tradition. "																		Northwest and Canada - Coastal	block	150	208/217/234		off							TOP	0.2		"TOP of the Late Northwest Coast (Developed Northwest Coast, Late Pacific, Late Prehistoric, Late Marine)"								"Late Northwest Coast (Developed Northwest Coast, Late Pacific, Late Prehistoric, Late Marine)"	1.5		"Chipped stone occurs throughout the region but is most common in the north. In the central and southern subregions, chipped stone points are more common. Ground-stone points are more common in the north, but ground-stone knives, clubs and large woodworking tools like celts, mauls, and adzes are found throughout the region. Bone was used to make anything from straight needles to bilaterally barbed harpoon points. Whale bone was used to make clubs in central and southern subregions. Rare and exotic materials such as obsidian, native copper, and drift iron (from shipwrecks) were held in high esteem, and ornaments or tools of these materials symbolised prestige and rank throughout the region. Wood was probably the most common material in use, although it is preserved less frequently than other substances. Wood was used for post and beam houses, large carved canoes, bent-wood boxes, and ceremonial masks for which the Northwest Coast indigenous people are famous. Follows the Middle Northwest Coast tradition, precedes the historic or proto-contact period. "								Middle Northwest Coast	5.5		"Considerable regional variablility in artefact forms, but chipped stone, ground stone, and ground bone, antler, and shell tools are found in all regions throughout this period. Pottery is absentThe most common type of site is shell midden. Shell midden sites dated to the later part of this period sometimes include rectangular house depression features of varying size, often arranged in rows. Follows the Early Northwest Coast traditon and precedes the Late Northwest Coast tradition that is generally associate with the development of ethnographic cultures.  "								Early Northwest Coast (Pebble Tool Tradition)	9.5		"Crude chopping tools made from fist-sized pebbles and associated with leaf shapped projectile points. Follows the Late Palaeo-Indian tradition in the region, precedes the Middle Northwest Coast tradition."								Paleo-Arctic	11		"Microblades, small, wedge-shaped prismatic cores, disk bifaces, leaf-shaped bifaces, burins. Precedes the Northern Archaic tradition. "																		Northwest and Canada - Western Subarctic	block	150	208/217/234		off							TOP	0.15		"TOP of the Proto-Athapaskan (Late Northern Archaic, Late Taltheilei in East, Athapaskan, Dene)"								"Proto-Athapaskan (Late Northern Archaic, Late Taltheilei in East, Athapaskan, Dene)"	2		"Fire-cracked rock, semisubterranean house in Alaska and northern Yukon, coarse ceramics in western Alaska, native copper artefacts, bone and antler arrowheads and spear prongs, bone and stone hide-processing tools (beamers, fleshers, end-of-bone scrappers, end scarpers bits, sotne slab thi-tho scrapers, awls), large pecked and ground adzes and picks, stone spearheads, flaked arrow tips. Follows the Northwest Microblade tradition, includes the early historic period."								Northwest Microblade	7		"Microblades, end scrappers, other beveled flake scrapers, side-notched, oblanceolate, and leaf-shapped spear points, square-based and elliptical flaked knives. Follows the Late Palaeoindian tradition, precedes the Proto-Athapaskan tradition."								Paleo-Arctic	11		"Microblades, small, wedge-shaped prismatic cores, disk bifaces, leaf-shaped bifaces, burins. Precedes the Northern Archaic tradition. The restricted placement of decoration means that the majority of the potsherds are plain. Small side-notched, corner-notched and triangular (late period) projectile points are common, as are linear flakes, ovid bifaced knives, and high frequencies of scrapers. Flakes for expedient use for tool blanks were commonly produced from small nodular cores, often employing a bipolar technique. Small quantities of copper awls, gorges, fishhooks, beada and cones have been recovered at many sites. Usually rare because of acid soils are artefacts made of bone, antler or teeth. But examples have been found of awls, gorges, and/or barbs for composite weapons, toggle-head and unilaterally barbed harpoons, beaver incisor knives and chisels, netting and snowshoe needles, and pottery stamping implements. Sites are usually small and suggest brief or seasonal occupations. Burial mounds are uncommon except in hte boundary waters area of northern Minnesota, southwestern Ontario, and southeastern Manitoba. Chronologically overlaps and then follows the Shield Archaic tradition; partially overlaps but largely precedes the historical period. "								TOP	4		Top of the Northern and Shield Archaic								Northern	6		"Leaf-shaped projectile points with wide and deep side notches and convex bases, changing to corner notched and lanceolate forms late in the tradition, unifacially flaked knives, end scrappers, and notched cobbles (presumably net sinkers). A general absence of microblade technology, present during both the preceding Palaeo-Arctic and the following Arctic Small Tool traditions, is also an important defining characteristic of the Northern Archaic tradidion. Follows the Palaeo-Archaic tradition, precedes the Western Arctic Small Tool and Eastern Arctic Small Tool traditions. "								Late Tundra	8		"Primarilly a blade and microblade industry, but at some sites there were associated bifacial projectile points/knives. With raw material common at most sites, there was not a great deal of care expended on removal of blades from cores with the exception of the Anangula blade site. When cores were reduced to microblade size in Kagati lake region, they began to assume more distinctive shapes. Microblades were conical to cylindrical in shape with multiple platforms.  Lance or leaf shaped projectile point or knife forms were produced at sites in the Kagati lake region with bifacial thinning flakes common in the debitage. Similar associations were noted at a number of other sites. Bifacial preforms were made from large flakes or derived from tabular slabs. At several sites of this tradition, burins are an important tool form. A greater array of tool forms such as fishing line weights, stone bowls, grinding pellets, ocher grinders, carved stone lamps, and small incised stone were recovered from the Anangula Blade site. Follows the Palaeoarctic and may have contributed in part to the following Northern Archaic tradition. "								Paleo-Arctic	11		"Microblades, small, wedge-shaped prismatic cores, disk bifaces, leaf-shaped bifaces, burins. Precedes the Northern Archaic tradition. "																		North America	:	N.Amer. overview	West Coast	Southwest	Plains	Eastern	_METACOLUMN_OFF															N.Amer. overview	:	Generalized N America ages																				West Coast	:	West Coast - Oregon	West Coast - Oregon Coast	West Coast - California Valley	West Coast - California Coast	West Coast - California Desert																Southwest	:	Southwest - Southern N. Triangular dart points, which give way to small, triangular arrow points. Use of steatite for beads, pendants, pipes, and bowls and dishes. Shell beads of Olivella and Detalia. Bone harpoon points and barbs for fishing gear. Hopper mortar bases, portable mortars, and bedrock mortars with pestles, shaped and cobble, with grinding slabs and mullers. House circular or oval semisubterranean pit houses. Large villages may have large dance houses or dance grounds. Follows the Early Sierra Nevada tradition, precedes contact with Europeans or Euro-Americans, which varies between 400 B.P. and 150 B.P depending on region."								Archaic Oregon Coast	10		"Lithic sites, shell middens, curvilinear pit houses, lanceolate and broadnecked projectile points. Bone harpoons and a diverse bone industry are also preasent. Ceramics and coiled basketry are both used. Burials are often in flexed position, and cremation is sometimes practised. Follows the Cosumnes tradition and precedes the historic period"								Cosumnes	3		Well-made projectile points with distinctive diagonal flaking across their faces; an extensive bone tool industry including serrated knives; distinctive shell and stone ornaments; burial usually in flexed position. Follows the Windmiller tradition and precedes the Hotchkiss tradition.								Windmiller (Early Horizon)	8		"The Windmiller peoples are the earliest known inhabitants of the California central valley. The made large leaf-shaped and stemmed projectile points, buried their dead with legs extended and pointing toward the west, often with grave goods and red ochre, and had a variety of distinctive shell and stone ornaments. Follows the Late Palaeoindian tradition (although it appears that the Windmiller peoples may have been the first in the California central valley), and precedes the Cosumnes tradition. "																		West Coast - California Coast	block	150	246/177/102		off							TOP	0.15		Late Southern California								Late Southern California	3		"Shell middens with sea mammals and fish remains; chert microblade drills; a great variety of Olivella biplicata beads, including saucer and other wall disk beads, lipped beads, and callus cup beads; many types of Halioris shell beads and ornaments; Megathura ring ornaments; clam cyclinder, tube, and globular beads; serpentine tubular beads; chlorite schist disk beads; large mammal tube beads; shell bead applique attached with asphaltum used to decorate bowls, mortars, and other items; shaped tapered, and cylindrical pestles; shaped globular mortars and flat-rimmed ""flower-pot"" mortars; J-shaped and circular shell fish-hooks; harpoon barbs; contracting stem dart points, small foliate and concave-based arrow points; small, side-notched arrow points in the San Diego region; steatite cooking vessels; bird bone whistles; deer tibia flutes; stone pipes; charm stones, bird stones, and incised tablets; sourthern California brown-ware; Yuman fired clay ""bow"" pipes and figurines. Shaped bone pins of ungulate metatarsals and pone pins made from birdbone shafts. Charm stones (plummet stones) with groove near one end for suspension, cogged stones, discoidals, doughnut stones, shaped stone spheres, and fired clay cyclinders. Unnotched round-based large projectile points, large projectile points with side notches or stems, compound fishooks and bone gorges. basin and flat metates, manos of variety of types, spherical mortars, and shaped cylindrical and tapered pestles. Flexed primaryinhumations often under cairns. Follows the Late Palaeoindian tradition, precedes the Late southern Californian tradition"																		West Coast - California Desert	block	150	246/177/102									TOP	0.15		Late Sierra Nevada								TOP	8		San Dieguito								San Dieguito	10		"A wide range of scrapers and bifaces, including unique crescent-shaped forms; leaf-shaped points or knives; stemmed projectile points; stone-bordered ""sleeping circles"". The oldest unambiguous cultural tradition in the Americas, it precedes the Late Palaeoindian and all subsequent traditions. "																		Southwest - Northern N. Great regional variation in settlement, architecture, and artefacts, but with general pattern of well-made black on while and redware coiled pottery. Follows the Basketmaker and precedes the Late Anasazi."								TOP	6		Late Paleo-Indian (Folsom)								Late Paleo-Indian (Folsom)	11		"Projectile points differentiate the Late Palaeoindian Tradition from others and also distinguish numerous regional subtraditions in Late Palaeoindian. Unique forms include Folsom, Agate Basin,Cody, and Dalton among others. All demonstrate extraordinary  craftsmanship and a care and precision in manufacture that often goes well beyond the functional perfomance  of the point. Although the Late Palaeoindians have been the subject of extensive research, their great antiquity and relatively simple material culture have yielded only modest insights into their culture. Follows the Early Palaeoindian tradition and precedes many of the subsequent traditions of the North and Middle America"																		"Southwest - Eastern Great Basin Arizona, Utah, Colorado, N. Settlements with mulitple dwellings include examples with hundreds of structures by the end of the Archaic (2900-1600 B.P.) in southern Arizona. Follows the Early Desert Archaic tradition, precedes the Late Desert Archaic tradition. Craft arts include worked marine shell, turquoise inlay, and clay effigies. Residential architechture consists of rectangular pit houses and surface structures constructed of puddled and coursed adobe and sometimes rock. Village layouts may include plazas and courtyards, either enclosed or surrounded by walled, quadrilateral compounds, with houses grouped in clusters. Public architecture consists of platform mounds, multistoried great houses, and towers, usually enclosed by compounds. The core area contains extensive irrigation systems. Burial practises include inhumation and cremation. Follows the Early Hohokam tradition and precedes the historic period. "								Late Desert Archaic (Proto-Numic)	1.6		"Small, triangular projectile points, usuallyside notched and sometimes with an additional basal notch. Diagnostic chipped stone tools include a variety of lanceolate points/knives. Other common artefacts are chipped-stone axes and gouges, T-shaped drills, and scrapers. The latter include unifacial or bifacial tools with hafting elements. A typical ground-stone inventory includes ovoid and rectangular manos and nutting stone, full or three quarter grooved axes, and celts. Sites are generally small, seasonally occupied or extensive, cyclically occupied camps. Follows the Late Palaeoindian tradition and precedes the Plains Woodland tradition. Rectangular wattle and daub houses with Middle Missouri, changing to circular earth lodges, often in fortified villages. Follows, but partly concurrent with, the Plains Woodland tradition, precedes the historic period. Follows the Middle High Plains Archaic tradition, precedes the High Plains Late Prehistoric tradition."								High Plains Early and Middle Archaic	8		The primary attribute distinguishing Early and Middle High Plains Archaic from the Late Palaeoindian tradition is the development of side-notched projectile points. A number of distinctive projectile point styles are diagnostic of the tradition. Pit houses and stone-filled pit ovens are also characteristic attributes of this tradition. The absence of ceramics is another diagnosic attribute. Follows the Late Palaeoindian tradition and precedes the Late High Plains Archaic tradition.																		Plains - Woodlands	block	150	246/177/102									TOP	0.2		Plains Woodland								Plains Woodland	2.5		"The fundamental diagnostic is cord-marked or cord-roughened pottery tempered with sand or crushed materials such as burned rock (grit), fired clay (grog), or bone. Plains Woodland components would often be difficult to distinguish from Plains Archaic  but for the presence of potsherds assignable to Plains Woodland wares and types. There are also several dozen named types of diagnostic projectile points that are used for cultural cross-dating. These diagnostic rands from a number of types of large and small corner-notched and side-notched dart points made diruing Early and Middle Plains Woodland to small notched and stemmed arrow points made during Late Plains Woodland. The occasional presence of artefacts reflecting Hopewellian influences also indicates Middle Plains Woodland. In some eastern portions of the plains, conical and linear earthen mounds were constructed, again reflecting influences emanating from mound-building cultures of the eastern woodlands. Follows Plains Archaic and precedes the Plains Village tradition or the historic period depending on region. "																		Plains Event	event		197/217/234		off						FAD												Plains horsemen	0.4		"Culture shift with European trade (horses, beads, guns) and wars after 1600"																		Mississippi Basin - Northern	block	150	246/177/102		off							TOP	0.35										Fort Ancient	1										TOP	1.7		Hopewell (mound builders)								Hopewell (mound builders)	2.1		"Thin, shell tempered pottery, often decorated with incising, stamping, or slipping. "								Eastern North America Late Archaic	6		"Stemmed and notched hafted bifaces; extensive ground-stone technology; diverse bone and antler technology; grooved axes; spear-thrower weights; soapstone vessels; fibre-tempered pottery in the southeast; ground-slate technology in the northeast; lapidary industry and baked clay objects in Poverty Point subtradition. Subsistence economy includes shellfish explotation in most subtraditions; domesticated cucurbita and weedy  annuals in midcontinent; marine mammals and swordfish in the far northeast. Storage pit technology in most subtraditions. "								Eastern North America Middle Archaic	8		"Stemmed hafted bifaces, cyclindrical and semilunate spear-thrower weights, and plummets, semilunar knives, and gouges in limited areas. Subsistence economy included shellfish exploitation late in the period. "								Eastern North America Early Archaic	10		"Three roughly time-sequential styles in point types are recognised: side-notched, corner-notched,  and bifurcate-based points. Side-notched points are small to medium sized bifaces with triangular and often alternately beveled blades. Moreover they exhibit small U-shaped  side-notcheds with squared or recurved basal edges. Corner-notched types are small to medium sized points exhibiting narrow but deep U-shaped corner notches. Point bases are straight to slightly excurvate and sometimes ground. Blade shapes are roughly triangular with straight to slightly excurvate blade margins that are commonly serrated and sometimes alternatelybeveled. Finally bifurcate forms are usually very small bifaces with short triangular blades and either shallow side-notched or short, stemmed bases that exhibit a shallow notch or bifurcation. "																		Eastern - Great Lakes Basin	block	150	246/177/102		off							TOP	0.5		Eastern North America Late Woodland								Proto-Iroquois (Algonquian and Iroquoian language cultures)	1		"Thin walled, usually grit tempered globular pots with necks and applied  collars with incised decoration and castellations, punctations, and/or notches. Cerros de Trincheras are defined by dry-laid masonry walls and stone terraces on isolated hills scattered over a wide region encompassing northern Sonora, southern Arizona, northwestern Chihuahua, and southwestern New Mexico spanning nearly 2500 years beginning about 3000 B.P. A Trincheras ceramic complex, including both plain and purple on red painted waters, is found over an area of north-central Sonora that only partially overlaps with the distribution of Cerros de Trincheras in time arid space. Trincheras ceramics are generally thought to equate time and to some extent style with the Preclassic Hohokam (1500-800 B.P.), although recent work suggests that this ceramic period contines as late as 450 B.P. Follows the Middle Desert Archaic tradition (North America), precedes the historic period. "								Highland Mesoamerican Early Preclassic	4		"Ceramics come in a wide range of foms including globular jars, bottles and tecomeates; decoration includes slips, grooving, carving, incising, and rocker stamping. Olmec design elements include double-line break, fire serpent, were-jaguar, paw-wing motifs. Handmade figurines are abundant, coming in a wide variety of forms, often with slanted or perforated eyes. Lithics including grinding stones, and chipped-stone tools such as knives, scrapers, and small, often stemless projectile points. Both chipping and core-blade techiques used to make cutting tools from obsidian. Certain jade and ceramic figures depict baby-faced individuals with downturned mouths. Rectangular wattle and daub houses, often arranged around open plazas. Settlement patterns often have a settlement hierarchy with civic-ceremonial platform mounds appearing in important sites.  Follows the Highland Mesoamerican Archaic and precedes the Late Highland Mesoamerican Preclassic."								Highland Mesoamerican Archaic	7		"Large, stemmed projectile points are found, but undiagnostic flake tools and crude scappers dominate archaeological assemblages. Ceramics were not made. Domesticated plants are first developed during this tradition and become fairly common by the end of the tradition. Sedentary communities also make their first appearance and become more common by the end of the tradition. Follows the Early Mesoamerican Archaic tradition and precedes the Early Highland Mesoamerican Preclassic tradition. "								Early Mesoamerican Archaic	9.6		"Chipped atlatl points with specialised stem or basal features, use of ground-and pecked-stoned tools, increase in carrying loops, nets, bags, and baskets for collecting, a variety of types of cords and weaving mats and textiles (plant), large scraping and chopping chipped tools, increase in woodworking, the development of seasonal scheduled subsistence system, micro-macroband settlement patterns, perhaps band social organization with some complex (burial) religious practice that were unique to Mesoamerica. Follows the Late Palaeoindian tradition and precedes all later Mesoamerican traditions. "																		Mexico - Western	block	150	206/187/222		off							TOP	0.48		West Mexico Postclassic								West Mexico Postclassic	1.1		"Red-slipped buff coloured pottery often decorated with white, red, and/or cream paint, with common use of resist decoration. Finer ceramic composition in general with many vessels having grey or tan paste. Polychrome pottery. Effigy supports head or feet, resembling deer hooves, snake heads, bat heads. Small triangular  projectile points. metal ornaments found in high-status contexts. Mixtec codices. Follows the Southern Mexican Highlands Classic tradition and preceeds the Contact/Colonial period. "								TOP	1.25										Southern Mexican Highland Classic	1.75		"Utilitarian ceramic vessels constitute the major diagnostic artefacts for this tradition, although there is considerable diversity in ceramic form, colour and decoration over the whole Southern Highlands Classic tradition. The valley of Oaxaca and adjacent areas are characterised by the development of what is called Zapotec ceramic tradition, featuring a preponderance of grey ware, which have coarse, roughly scraped exterior surfaces. Other diagnostics of the Zapotec ceramic tradition include a bowl with external incised designs (Early Classic) and anthropomorphic vessels referred to as Zapotec urns (Early and Late Classic, as well as other periods. Bowls with bean applique design and hollow slab support, as well as thin orange bowls; found exclusively at Monte Alban. In the Nochiztlan Valley, and adjacent areas of the Mixteca region, have ceramics with tan, fine cream, rust or orange pastes. Houses typically consisted of one or more rectanglur or square rooms, often built around an open central patio. Households of higher social position had rooms that were elevated several feet above the patio. In other cases, the whole house was elevated on platforms, with a staircase for access. Burials were placed in and around houses, some in slablined graves or roofed tombs. Tombs varied from rectangular to cruciform. Tombs of important people might include stone-lined niches for offerings and elaborate mural scenes and writing rendered in painted and carved stone. Of the public buildings, many categories were on elevated platforms, such as two-chambered temples and civic buildings. Both were built in relation to an ajoining plaza, which could be open or enclosed by multiple compounds. Additionally, carved stones and murals that use Zapotec writing and other symbols are diagnostic. These murals usually consist  of scenes potraying elite and are accompanied by texts that include hierogylphic and phoetic written elements. Writing aimed at public communication declined by the Late Classic period. Follows the Late Highland Mesoamerican Preclassic tradition, preceedes the Postclassic Southern Mexican Highlands tradition."								Gulf Coast Classic (Classic Veracruz)	2.05		"Diverse regional ceramic types, many characterised by bichrome and polychrome painting, primarily in red, black and white, and especially in the south, untempered orange and grey pastes. Several figurine styles, including earlier modeled sharp-nosed narigudo figurines, and, later, mold-made hollow, white-slipped figurines. Animal figures with wheels and smiling faces figures are particularly distinctive elements of the Gulf Coast Classic. A tradition of large, lifelike ceramic and unfired clay statues was centered in the lower Papaloapan basin. Figurines and statues are often detailed with asphaltum paint. Stone carving includes representations of ball-game paraphernalia: U-shaped yokes, hachas in the form of thing human faces, and long, curved palmas. Typical of north-central Veracruz is a low-relief sculptural style emphasizing wide, interlaced scrolls. In south-central and southern Veracruz, Protoclassic and Early Classic, stone stalae sometimes bear epi-Olmec hieroglyphic texts and Long Count dates. Ceramic and architechtural traits derived from Central Mexico define a Middle Classic horizon in many sites. The ceramic traits include cylindrical tripod jars, articulated figurines with triangular heads, small rectangular or ellipitcal artefacts with one or two holes (candeleros), Talud-tablero architecture appears at Matacapan in modified form El Tajin and related sites. Settlements include primary, seconary, and on occasion, tertiary centers as well as hamlets, villages, and special purpose extractive sites. Centers contain pyramidal temples, range structures, ball courts, and broad platforms arranged around plazas. These structures are typically stone faced over a rubble core in the north and primarily of earthen consturction in the south. Follows the Olmec tradition, precedes the Central Mexico Postclassic tradition. Encompasses the Protoclassic, Early Classic, Middle Classic, Late Classic or Epiclassic, and Early Postclassic periods. "								Olmec	3.4		"Monolithic basalt sculpture in the form of heads and thrones; sculptures with feline themes; coarse and medium black wares decorated with excised motifs (crosses, abstract felines, and dragons); fine white potter; distinctively carved jade objects. Massive earthen constructions in the form of pyramids and extensively modified terraces. Overlaps the Early Highland Mesoamerican Preclassic tradition and precedes the Gulf Coast Classic tradition. "								Highland Mesoamerican Early Preclassic	4		"Ceramics come in a wide range of foms including globular jars, bottles and tecomeates; decoration includes slips, grooving, carving, incising, and rocker stamping. Olmec design elements include double-line break, fire serpent, were-jaguar, paw-wing motifs. Handmade figurines are abundant, coming in a wide variety of forms, often with slanted or perforated eyes. Lithics including grinding stones, and chipped-stone tools such as knives, scrapers, and small, often stemless projectile points. Both chipping and core-blade techiques used to make cutting tools from obsidian. Certain jade and ceramic figures depict baby-faced individuals with downturned mouths. Rectangular wattle and daub houses, often arranged around open plazas. Settlement patterns often have a settlement hierarchy with civic-ceremonial platform mounds appearing in important sites.  Follows the Highland Mesoamerican Archaic and precedes the Late Highland Mesoamerican Preclassic."								Highland Mesoamerican Archaic	7		"Large, stemmed projectile points are found, but undiagnostic flake tools and crude scappers dominate archaeological assemblages. Ceramics were not made. Domesticated plants are first developed during this tradition and become fairly common by the end of the tradition. Sedentary communities also make their first appearance and become more common by the end of the tradition. Follows the Early Mesoamerican Archaic tradition and precedes the Early Highland Mesoamerican Preclassic tradition. "								Early Mesoamerican Archaic	9.6		"Chipped atlatl points with specialised stem or basal features, use of ground-and pecked-stoned tools, increase in carrying loops, nets, bags, and baskets for collecting, a variety of types of cords and weaving mats and textiles (plant), large scraping and chopping chipped tools, increase in woodworking, the development of seasonal scheduled subsistence system, micro-macroband settlement patterns, perhaps band social organization with some complex (burial) religious practice that were unique to Mesoamerica. Follows the Late Palaeoindian tradition and precedes all later Mesoamerican traditions. "																		Mexico - Central	block	150	206/187/222		off							TOP	0.429		Central Mexico Postclassic								Central Mexico Postclassic	1.3		"Pottery varied over time and area, and major styles and traditions included Coyotlatelco, Mazapan, Toltec, Choluteca, Matlazinca, Tlahuica, and Aztec. Pottery was made in a variety of forms, often using molds;  common forms include bowls, jars, basins, ladle-censers, griddles, and tripod dishes with scored bottoms used to grind chiles and tomatoes. Other ceramics included salt wares for transporting salt, moldmade figurines and spindle whorls, and small tripod bowls to support spindles during cotton spinning. Pottery with polished painted red surfaces was common. Decorated serving wares increased in frequency in the Late Postclassic, including Aztec III Black on Orange that was manufactured in multiple production centers in the Basin of Mexico and widely exchanged. Prismatic obsidian blades made from polyhedral cores were the common cutting tool. Postclassic monumental architectural forms included ball courts, skull racks, and colonnaded halls. Terraced pyramids with double stairsways and twin temples developed in the Middle Postclassic. Settlement patterns were complex and hierarchical, and during the Postclassic they underwent cycles of centralization and fragmentation. The Late Postclassic is distinguished by the largest population of any prehispanic period in Central Mexico, a high density of rural, as well as urban, settlements, and a hierarchy of urban centers, including city-state capitals, conquest state capitals, and the surpraregional Aztec imperial city of Tenochtitlan-Tlatelolco. Follows the Central Mexico Classic period, precedes the historic period."								Central Mexico Classic (Teotihuacan)	2.15		"There is a distinctive loack ceramic tradition, most elements of which do not spread far beyond the basin of Mexico, including storage, cooking, and serving types such as ollas, basins, caraters, and cazuelas, flat-bottomed serving bowls with outcurving sides and nubin supports, and a distinctive utility ware called San Martin Orange. Composite ""theater"" type censers of the Teotihuacan inspiration are well known in highland Pacific coastal Guatemala, and distinctive little twin-chambered censers called candeleros are present in some numbers at Matacapan in southern Veracruz and occur sparsely elsewhere in Mesoamerica. Direct rim cyclinder tripod vases were important at Teotihuacan after about 1700 B.P., but the form probably originated in the Gulf lowlands and has many variants. Thin Orange is a distinctive ware made in southern Puebla. Teotihuacan also has a distinctive architectural style. Civic-ceremonial structures and even platforms in residential compounds often consist of a sloping apron (talud) at the base, surmounted by a vertical recessed panel (tablero). Higher structures often have two to seven stacked talu-tablero levels, each smaller than the one below, so that the overall effect is pyramidal. Some pyramids, however, were built wholly or partly of single or stacked talud elements, without verticle panels. Residential structures at least after about 1750 B.P., is composed of very distinctive walled compounds, each containing several single-story apartments. Each apartment is typically focused on a small central patio. Often a larger patio with a central altar seems to pertain to the compound as a whole. Especially around the main patio, there were often low talud-tablero platforms supporting rooms with porticoes facing the patio. Follows the Late Highland Mesoamerican Preclassic tradition and precedes the Central Mexico Postclassic tradition. "																		Mexico - Highland	block	150	206/187/222		off							TOP	1.6		Highland Mesoamerican Late Preclassic								Highland Mesoamerican Late Preclassic	2.6		"Calendrical and other hieroglyphic inscripitions in stone are one form of monumental art. Both well-made and more crudely manufactured pottery of varying pastes, tempering, and firing treatments, some decorated with molding, incising, excising, raspada, slipping, and/or paint. Highly variable range of functional categories and pottery vessel forms. Rectangular houses vary from modest single-room structures of wattle and daub of adobe brick, with associated exterior work areas and features, to large multiroom masonry dwellings atop platform mounds and arranged around tightly enclosed courtyards. Settlement patterns include a hierarchy of settlement sizes, a range of site types based on monumental and high status residential architecture, and the emergence of the first true cities in Mesoamerica. Follows the Early Highland Mesoamerican Preclassic tradition and precedes the Central Mexico Classic and Southern Mexico Highlands Classic traditions. "								Highland Mesoamerican Early Preclassic	4		"Ceramics come in a wide range of foms including globular jars, bottles and tecomeates; decoration includes slips, grooving, carving, incising, and rocker stamping. Olmec design elements include double-line break, fire serpent, were-jaguar, paw-wing motifs. Handmade figurines are abundant, coming in a wide variety of forms, often with slanted or perforated eyes. Lithics including grinding stones, and chipped-stone tools such as knives, scrapers, and small, often stemless projectile points. Both chipping and core-blade techiques used to make cutting tools from obsidian. Certain jade and ceramic figures depict baby-faced individuals with downturned mouths. Rectangular wattle and daub houses, often arranged around open plazas. Settlement patterns often have a settlement hierarchy with civic-ceremonial platform mounds appearing in important sites.  Follows the Highland Mesoamerican Archaic and precedes the Late Highland Mesoamerican Preclassic."								Highland Mesoamerican Archaic	7		"Large, stemmed projectile points are found, but undiagnostic flake tools and crude scappers dominate archaeological assemblages. Follows the Early Mesoamerican Archaic tradition and precedes the Early Highland Mesoamerican Preclassic tradition. The Greater Nicoya ceramic complex had some important type-variety distinctions between the Rivas and Nicoya peninsula subtraditions, primarily white slipped ceramics in Rivas and tan-orange slipped ceramics in Nicoya from 1200-750 B.P. Pertroglyphs are found in areas with natural boulders, naturally formed stone platforms, or rock outcrops. Obsidian flakes and blades were imported from 1200 B.C. on, from Honduras to Guatemala, and are more common in the Rivas subtradition than in the Nicoya peninsula, and in both subtraditions are combined with expedient local chert, jasper, chalcedony, and basalt-andesite industries. Because of historical, environmental, and geographical differences between the two subtraditions, other dimensions of the cultural tradition are better characterised as being more typical of one subtradition or the other. Primary settlements in the Rivas subtradition is near the shores of lake Nicaragua and Managua, whereas Nicoya peninsula subtradition they are along the coast and in Tempisque river valley; jade artefacts are most common in the Nicoya subtradition and obsidian in the Rivas. Follows the Late Northwest South American Littoral tradition, precedes the historic period. "								Coahuilan	8		"Well-developed fibre industry, a variety of stone tools with chronological implication, rock shelter and open occupation sites, regionally specific petroglyph and pictograph styles. Follows the Late Palaeoindian tradition and precedes the historic period. Encompasses the period from the end of the Pleistocene until European contact and subsumes the early Cienagas complex, and later Jora comlex, as well as the mortuary Mayan complex. "																		Central Amerca - Eastern	block	150	206/187/222		off							Top	0.479		Top of Mayan								Postclassic Maya	1.1		"Major attributes include architechtural features, settlement characteristics, and pottery types. The architechtural features include both public and domestic constructions. New public architechtural features include colonnades halls, many with beam and plaster roofs. Round public buildings like the observatory at Chichen Itza are more common, although some have been dated to the Late and Terminal Classic Maya. I-shaped ball courts and four stairway temples are other features more common in the Postclassic than Classic. A major common feature in many areas is the building or range-type structure with C-chaped benches. Adoratorios and small square shrines are also major Postclassic architectural features. Pottery styles tend to be regionally specific. Perhaps the most common ceramic markers are the widespread use of both spiked hourglass unslipped censers (Chehach-Hunacti composite) and effigy censers (Chen Mul molded). Stylistic characteristics include bell-shaped and tripod feet in red-slipped dishes and bowls. Widely traded serving wares that date to this horizon include Parbellon modelled-carved and other fine paste orange wares dating to the Terminal Classic and early Postclassic and Silho and other fine orangewares dating to the Postclassic. Another imported time horizon marker is Plumbate ware imported from the Guatemalan-Chiapan borderland. Follows the Classic Maya tradition, precedes the historic period. "								Classic Maya	1.85		"(Lowlands): Masonry architecture with corbeled vaulting, carved stone monuments (stelae and alters) with calendrical dates, hieroglyphic writing (folded books, text on monuments, buildings, and artefacts), polychrome pottery. Follows the Preclassic Maya tradition and precedes the Postclassic Maya tradition. "								Preclassic Maya	3.8		"Simple monochrome and bichrome pottery comes in a range of forms, including composite bowls, globular jars, tripod vessels, and effigy whistling vessels. Decorative techniques include fluting, grooving, punctation, rocker-stamping,  and incised geometric designs. Handmade figurines come in a variety of forms, often with very crude arms and legs. Chert and obsidian were processed into a variety of knives, scrapers, and stemless or tapered-stem projectile points. Residences are generallyof rectangular wattle-and-daub construction; at times they occur in clustered groups of three to five structures arranged around an open plaza. Follows the Lowland Mesoamerican Archaic tradition, preceeds the Classic Maya tradition. "								Lowland Mesoamerican Archaic	7		"Chert microblades, Lowe dart points, coastal shell middens, fibre-tempered pottery. Follows the Early Mesoamerican Archaic tradition, precedes the Preclassic Maya tradition. "																		Central America event	event		197/217/234		off						FAD												Terminal Classic	1.2										Late Classic	1.4										Early Classic	1.85									LAD												Terminal Classic	1.1										Late Classic	1.2										Early Classic 	1.4									EVENT												Spanish conquests 1521-1535 AD. 	0.479																				Caribbean - Mainland Coast	block	150	206/187/222		off							TOP	0.5										Chiriqui	1.2		"Three-colour (black, red and cream) polychrome pottery, two (black and red) and three colour (black, red and cream) negative pottery, large perfectly round balls, a very thin, well-fired ceramic called biscuit, peg-base stone statues, triangular stone projectile points, goldwork, funerary stone pillars, stone cist graves. Saladoid series is composed of well made pottery vessels including complex shapes decorated with red, black, white on red, and polychrome painting; zoned-incised zoomorphic adornos; strap and loop handles. Small zoomorphic stone pendants and beads made from exotic materials. Follows and displaces Late Northwest South American Littoral. Develops as part of Early Amazonean tradition. Also diagnostic of late Manteno culture are large burial urns; decorated ceramic spindle whorls; copper-bronze implements, ornaments, and axe-monies; gold and silver ornaments and vessels; and stone scultptures including U-shaped  stone seats. Manteno peoples constructed deep shaft tombs, earthen platforms, agricultural terraces, wells and water catchment facilities, and buildings with stone foundations. Manteno, assigned to the integration period in the widely used archaeological chronology of Ecuador, follows the Bahia culture, was contemporary with the final portion of the Central Coastal Chiefdoms tradition, and was terminated at the European contact. "								Ecuadorian Highlands	1.5		"The Late period in Carchi/Narino is marked by the appearance of a distinctive resist-decorated ware, along with a distinctive far form, the Botijuela, and a new goldworking tradition. The earliest circular house foundations in this region also date to this period. In Imbabura/Pichincha region, the Late period is marked by the beginning of mouns construction. The break between middle and late periods in the Chimborazo/Bolivar subtradition seems to be marked by the appearance of incised decoration and perhaps anthropomorphic jars. There is no such clear distinguishing feature in Canar/Azuay subtradition. however, the Late period in the Loja Regional subtradition is distinguished by the appearance of corrugated wares that seem to reflect Amazonian penetration of a region formerly occupied by highland peoples. Follows and overlaps with the Andean Regional Development tradition, precedes the historic period. Generally corresponds to the ""Integration Period"" in the standard chronology used by both Ecuadorian and international scholars. In this chronology, the Integration period followed the ""Regional Development Period"" (1500-2500 B.P.) and preceded the Inca conquest that began about 500 B.P. "								Central Coast Regional Chiefdoms	2.5		"Elaborate hollow ceramic figurines depicting high-status personages in ritual attire; large central communities with flat-topped pyramidal mounds (tolas); rectangular and square ceremonial structures; miniatures ceramic models (manquettas) of these structures. Diagnostic pottery includes shallow polypod bowls and tall pedestal bowls or cups (compoteras). Follows the Early Chibcha tradition and precedes the Mateno tradition. Falls within the Regional Developmental period in the traditional Ecuadorian periodisation scheme. It follows the Late Formative period (Chorrera culture) and precedes the so-called Integration period . However, the ending date of 1500 B.P. is variable throughout Ecuador, as several ehthic groups continued until the conquest with little or no ""integration"" into larger policies. "								Late Northwest South American Littoral	3		TOP								Late Northwest South American Littoral	5.5		"Simple stone tools made of local and imported materials. Grinding and chopping stones, edge-utilised flakes. Large shells were modified to serve as possible digging implements, bowls, or spoons. Smaller shell artefacts included beads and pendants. Pointed tools made of animal bone have also been found. In parts of Ecuador and Colombia, this period was marked by the appearance and/or continued elaboration of ceramics, with a diverse array of forms and decorative techniques, including figurines. Follows the Early Northwest South American Littoral tradition, precedes the Early Chibcha tradition. "																		NW South America - Sothern Middle America	block	150	206/187/222		off							TOP	0.5		Paya/ Late Chibcha (Columbia)								Late Chibcha (Columbia)	1.2		"Goldwork of outstanding craftsmanship, often in unique anthropomorphic and animorphic forms.Distinctive ceramics with a great deal of regional variation, including tripod drinking vessels from eastern Costa Rica, fine biscuit ware jars from western Panama, and long necked jars from Sabana de Bogat in Columbia. Large settlements with open plazas and elevated platforms for cermonies or residences. Follows the Early Chibcha tradition and precedes the historic period. "								Paya	1.5		"Polychrome ceramics, often red and black on orange; chipped T-shaped axes. Follows the Early Chibcha tradition, precedes the historic period. Fine red-slipped and smoked pottery. Stone columns sometimes with carved human and/or animal figures and cobblestone pavements, but at contiguous Sitio Conte and El Cano - probably the tradition's topranked political and ceremonial center. Extremely varied mortuary practices. Burials in subterranean graves and earthen mounds, but only simple nonperishable grave architecture. Unassuming dwellings with clay floors and thatched roofs. Stone-lined ovens in circles. Little landscape modification, but some stone-walled precincts and terraces. Cotton and bark fabrics, but no information on designs. Follows the Early Chibcha tradition and precedes the historic period. In the local sequence, period covered is generally referred to as Middle Ceramic B to Late Ceramic E. Overlaps completely with the Paya and ends about 400 BP (-1600 AC)"								Early Chibcha (Columbia)	3.5		"Ceramics with plastic decoration, greenstone ornaments, gold ornaments, flying panel metates, lime containers. Large shells were modified to serve as possible digging implements, bowls or spoons. Smaller shell artefacts included beads and pendants. Pointed tools made of animal bone were used, possibly as projectile points. Red hematite was also employed. The latter part of the period saw the emergence of fiber and sand-tempered  pottery decorated with incision and zoomorphic adornments, in the interior hills of Caribbean Colombia. Follows the Late Palaeoindian tradition and precedes the Late Northwest South American Littoral tradition. Rectangular structures, often with gabled roofs and trapezoidal doors and niches. Limited repertoire of highly standarized polychrome pottery vessels typically decorated with geometric design, the tall-necked jar known as the aribalo being the quitenssential Inca vessel form. Planned provincial administrative centres with standard architecture features, including a central ceremonial platform, storage facilities, a hours for ""chosen women"", and a large rectangular halls. Carved or otherwise modified natural rock outcrops. Large-scale agricultural terracing, hydraulic systems, and road networks. Miniature human and camellid forms cast in metal. Geometric design blocks. Follows the Andean Regional States tradition and precedes the historic period ushered in by the Spanish Conquest in 468 B.P."								Aymara Kingdom (Lake Titicaca) 	0.9		"Fortified settements know as pukaras, burial towers called chulapas, Sillustani and Collao pottery types.
